Taharah — Purification
advanced
12 min
The Hanafi Ruling on Wiping over Splints (Jabirah): Obligation and Conditions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the obligation level of wiping over splints and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on wiping over splints, identify the mufta bihi, and apply the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
Wiping over a splint is wajib/fard, not a timed substitute like khuffs, and does not require prior purity.

When a broken limb is set in a splint (jabirah) and cannot be washed or wiped directly, the Hanafi school prescribes wiping over the splint. However, the precise legal status—whether it is fard, wajib, or mustahab—is a subject of intra-madhhab dispute. This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya refines the ruling by examining the proofs, the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and the practical conditions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: wiping over the splint is obligatory. Note that there are three views: fard (Sahibayn), wajib (Abu Hanifa), and mustahab (some later scholars).
The mufta bihi is fard as a precaution, but the 'sahih' view is wajib.
2
Identify the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Contrast Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the binding level and why the difference matters (fard invalidates prayer if omitted; wajib does not).
Al-Razi's distinction: if the wound could be washed if exposed, wiping is obligatory; otherwise not.
3
Analyse the proofs
Examine the dalil: the hadith of the Prophet wiping over his bandage and ordering Ali to wipe over his splint, and the Quranic principle of removing hardship (5:6).
The hadith are weak but supported by consensus (ijma) and the Qur'an.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the splint is omitted without excuse, is the prayer valid? Under Abu Hanifa: yes (wajib); under Sahibayn: no (fard).
The mufta bihi follows Sahibayn for caution.
5
Learn the conditions
The splint need not be tied in a state of purity; wiping is not limited to a period; it suffices to wipe once over most of the splint; falling off before healing does not invalidate.
Wiping over a splint is treated as an original (asl), not a pure substitute (badal).

غير واجب لأنّ فرض الأصل قد سقط، فلا يتعلق بما قام مقامه كمقطوع القدم إذا لبس الخف، وهذا يفيد أن المراد بقوله : فالمسح واجب الفرض لا الواجب المصطلح عليه .اهـ. وقال الصيرفي : هذا أحسن الأقوال اهـ وإذا الصحيح) وفي التتمة يفتى وفي الخلاصة، وعليه الفتوى وإليه جنح صاحب الهداية واختار في الكنز الاستيعاب . بين عصابة المقتصد ونحوه إن ضره حلها تبعاً للضرورة لئلا يسري الماء فيضر الجراحة وإن لم يضر الحل حلها وغسل الصحيح ومسح الجريح وإن ضره المسح تركه (والمسح) على الجبيرة ونحوها (كالغسل) لما تحتها وليس بدلاً بخلاف الخف لأنه بدل محض (فلا يتوقت) مسح الجبيرة (بمدة) لكونه أصلاً ولا يشترط لصحة المسح (شد الجبيرة) ونحوها (على طهر) دفعاً للحرج (ويجوز مسح جبيرة إحدى الرجلين مع غسل الأخرى) لكونه أصلاً (ولا يبطل المسح بسقوطها قبل البرء) لقيام العذر والجنابة والحدث سواء فيها ويجوز مسح قوله : (لئلا يؤدي إلى فساد الجراحة لأنه يحتاج إلى الإستقصاء في إيصال البلل إلى جميع أجزاء الخرقة ونحوها فيؤدي إلى نفوذ البلة إلى الجراحة فيفسدها قوله : (وكفى المسح الخ) هو الأصح كما في الذخيرة وغيرها وعليه مشى في مختارات النوازل لأنه لو كلف غسل ذلك الموضع ربما تبتل العصابة، وتنفذ البلة إلى موضع الفصد فيتضرر، وقيل: يفترض إيصال الماء إلى الموضع الذي لم تستره العصابة لأنه باد أي ظاهر قوله (ونحوه) كخرقة الجراحة والقرحة والكي، والكسر لأنّ الضرورة تشمل الكل قوله : (إن ضره حلها) قال في هداية الناطفي : ليس عليه أن يغسل ما تحت العصابة من غير موضع الجراحة إن كان حل العصابة يضر بالجراحة، وإن كان لا يضر حلها ولكن نزعها عن موضع الجراحة يضر بالجراحة، فإنّ عليه أن يحلها، ويغسل ما تحتها إلى أن يبلغ موضعاً يضر بالجراحة ثم يشد العصابة ويمسح على موضع الجراحة .اهـ. قوله : (وإن ضره المسح (تركه إتفاقاً دفعاً للحرج لأن الغسل سقط بالعذر فالمسح أولى وفي المبتغي بالغين ومن كان جميع رأسه مجروحاً لا يجب المسح عليه لأن المسح بدل عن الغسل، ولا بدل له وقيل يجب اهـ. قال في البحر: والصواب هو الوجوب، وقوله: المسح بدل عن الغسل غير صحيح، بل المسح على الرأس أصل بنفسه لا بدل لا كما يخفي اهـ وهو مخالف لما في الرهبانية والقنية من سقوطه، وقد يقال في التوفيق : إن كان الواجب غسل الرأس كما في الغسل، وضره المسح سقط وإن كان الواجب المسح كما في الوضوء، وضره لا يسقط، ويمسح على العصابة لأنّ المسح في الأول بدل وفي الثاني أصل ويحرر، ثم رأيت في التنوير وشرحه من به وجه رأس لا يستطيع محدثاً، ولا غسله الفيض عن غريب الرواية يتيمم، وأفتى قاري الهداية أنه يسقط عنه فرض مسحه، جنباً ففي ! عليه جبيرة ففي مسحها ،قولان، وكذا يسقط غسله فيمسحه ولو على جبيرة إن لم يضره وإلا سقط أصلاً وجعل عادماً لذلك العضو حكماً كما في المعدوم حقيقة اهـ. قوله: (وليس بدلاً) أي محضاً، بل نزل منزلة الأصل لعدم القدرة عليه وإن كان في نفسه بدلاً بدليل أنه لا يجوز عند القدرة على الغسل قوله : (فلا يتوقف بمدة) أي معلومة، بل بالبرء. قوله: (دفعاً للحرج) أي الحاصل بغسلها الضرر قوله: (لكونه أصلاً)، أي فلا جامعاً بين الأصل، والبدل. يصير قوله : (بسقوطها قبل البرء ولو في الصلاة وبرأ من باب نفع وتعب ويأتي في لغة كقرب وإذا معه ولو
Two sticks from palm branches wrapped with paper and tied on the broken limb, and he cannot wash the limb with cold or hot water - and it is said that using hot water is not obligatory - and he cannot wipe it, then wiping is obligatory according to the correct view, once, in the correct view, and it is said: it should be repeated except for the head, and it is recommended in a narration, and it is said: it is obligatory because the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to wipe over his bandage, and when the forearm of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) was broken on the day of Uhud or the day of Khaybar, the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered him to wipe over the splints, and wipe over most of what the limb is tied with. This is to avoid causing harm to the wound by covering it completely, and wiping over what appears from the body suffices. The correct [view] is based on ahad [reports] by agreement. 'Splint' (jabirah) is from 'jabr' meaning repair, as in al-Misbah, so named as an omen of good, just as a place of destruction is called 'mafaza' (desert). His saying: 'wrapped with paper' i.e., for example. His saying: 'it is said that using hot water is not obligatory' - it is decided in al-Siraj to avoid hardship. In al-Bahr, the first is apparent. His saying: 'and he cannot wipe it' - said in al-Bada'i: if wiping directly on the wound does not harm it, it is not permissible to wipe except on the wound itself, and it is not permissible to wipe over the splint because its permissibility is due to an excuse, and there is no excuse. His saying: 'according to the correct view' i.e., from the Imam, so prayer is valid without it because the obligation is only established by definitive evidence, and what is narrated is a khabar (report) which only necessitates action but not knowledge, so we ruled the obligation of wiping practically, and we did not rule the invalidity of prayer due to its omission without excuse because the ruling of invalidity goes back to knowledge, and this evidence does not provide it. This is chosen in al-Fath, and in al-Sharh it is relied upon. His saying: 'and it is said: it should be repeated except for the head' - for the head its wiping is not repeated. It is better that the Sharh adds the word 'once' to contrast with 'it is said: it is repeated'. And if a quarter of the head remains, he wipes it, otherwise he wipes over the bandage, as stated by al-Sayyid. It might be said: why is wiping the healthy part not specified? And he said: he completes the obligation by wiping over the bandage. His saying: 'and it is said' that it is obligatory is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). In al-Idah, the fatwa is according to their view as a precaution. In al-Bahr, the gist is that the authentication differed regarding its being farḍ or wajib, and I have not seen anyone authenticating its being mustahab according to his view. Al-Razi made a distinction: if what is under the splint, if exposed, could be washed, then wiping is obligatory because the obligation pertains to the original, so it pertains to what stands in its place, like wiping over khuffs; but if what is under it, if exposed, cannot be washed, then wiping over it is not obligatory because the obligation of the original has lapsed, so it does not pertain to what stands in its place, like the one with a severed foot when wearing a khuff. This indicates that what is meant by 'wiping is obligatory' is farḍ, not the technical wajib. Al-Sayrafi said: this is the best of opinions. So if you know what was mentioned, you know that attributing obligation to the two companions is not appropriate. His saying: 'because the Prophet...' is evidence for the principle of wiping as in al-Sharh. His saying: 'he used to wipe over his bandage' when Ibn Qumay'a hit him on the Day of Uhud. What is reported in this chapter of ahadith is weak but used for comfort. In al-Halabi, the weakness of the hadith does not harm us after the consensus of the mujtahids based on clear evidence: Allah's saying: 'Allah does not intend to make difficulty for you' [5:6]. His saying: 'it is the correct view' - in al-Tatimma, fatwa is given accordingly, and in al-Khulasa, fatwa is upon it, and the author of al-Hidaya inclined to it, and in al-Kanz, al-Isti'ab (covering the majority) is chosen. He said: (between the bandage of the frugal one and its like, if its removal harms him, it is left due to necessity so that water does not seep and harm the wound. If removal does not harm him, he removes it, washes the healthy part, and wipes over the wounded part. If wiping harms him, he leaves it.) (And wiping) over the splint and its like (is like washing) what is under it, and it is not a substitute, unlike the khuff because it is a pure substitute. (So wiping over a splint is not limited) to a specific time (period) because it is an original. (Tightening the splint) and its like (in a state of purity) is not a condition for the validity of wiping, to avoid hardship. (It is permissible to wipe over a splint on one foot while washing the other) because it is an original. (The wiping is not invalidated by its falling off before healing) because the excuse is established. Major or minor impurity are the same regarding it. It is permissible to wipe... His saying: 'so that it does not lead to spoiling the wound' because it requires thoroughness in delivering moisture to all parts of the cloth etc., leading to moisture penetrating the wound and spoiling it. His saying: 'and wiping is sufficient, etc.' - this is the most correct as in al-Dhakhira and others, and according to it is proceeded in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil, because if he were obligated to wash that spot, perhaps the bandage would become soaked and moisture would reach the phlebotomy spot causing harm. It is said: it is obligatory to deliver water to the spot not covered by the bandage because it is exposed i.e., apparent. His saying: 'and its like' like the cloth of a wound, ulcer, cautery and fracture because necessity encompasses all. His saying: 'if removal harms him' - said in Hidaya al-Natifi: he does not have to wash what is under the bandage from other than the wound site if removing the bandage harms the wound; but if removal does not harm him but removing it from the wound site harms the wound, then he must remove it, wash what is under it until he reaches a spot that harms the wound, then tie the bandage and wipe over the wound site. His saying: 'and if wiping harms him, he leaves it' - unanimously, to avoid hardship, because washing is dropped due to excuse, so wiping is more appropriate. In al-Muntaghā (with ghayn), whoever has his entire head wounded does not have to wipe because wiping is a substitute for washing, and it has no substitute; it is said: it is obligatory. In al-Bahr: the correct view is obligation, and his saying 'wiping is a substitute for washing' is incorrect; rather, wiping on the head is originally an obligation in itself, not a substitute, as is not hidden. This contradicts what is in al-Rahbaniyya and al-Quniyya about it being dropped. It may be said in reconciliation: if the obligation is washing the head, as in ghusl, and wiping harms, it is dropped; if the obligation is wiping, as in wudu, and it harms, it is not dropped, and he wipes over the bandage because wiping in the first is a substitute, in the second an original. This needs verification. Then I saw in al-Tanwir and its commentary: one who has a face or head who cannot [wipe] as a minor impure, nor wash it - in Gharib al-Riwaya he performs tayammum; Qari al-Hidaya gave a fatwa that the obligation of wiping is dropped from him. For a junub, if he has a splint, there are two opinions regarding wiping it. Likewise, his washing is dropped and he wipes even over a splint if it does not harm him; otherwise it is dropped entirely, and he is deemed as lacking that limb legally, as if it were truly non-existent. His saying: 'and it is not a substitute' i.e., not a pure substitute; rather, it is treated as the original due to inability to access it, even if in itself it is a substitute, as evidenced by the fact that it is not permissible when one is able to wash. His saying: 'so it is not limited to a period' i.e., a known period, but [lasts] until healing. His saying: 'to avoid hardship' i.e., the hardship caused by washing it [causing] harm. His saying: 'because it is an original' i.e., so there is no combination between the original and the substitute. His saying: 'by its falling off before healing' even if during prayer, and 'healed' is from the pattern na'fa and ta'iba, and it comes in language like qaruba... and when with him even if

Commentary
This passage is from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah, critically refining the rulings on wiping over splints (jabirah) and bandages in the Hanafi school. The author opens by defining the scenario: a broken limb set with splints made from palm branches wrapped with paper. The key ruling is that if a person cannot wash the limb with cold or hot water due to injury, and cannot even wipe the skin directly, then wiping over the splint is obligatory (wajib) according to the correct view (sahih) once. There is a difference of opinion: some say it should be repeated except for the head; some say it is recommended (mustahab); others say it is farḍ. The author then presents the proof: the Prophet (peace be upon him) wiped over his bandage and ordered Ali to wipe over his splint. The author notes that these hadith are weak but are supported by consensus and the Quranic verse 'Allah does not intend to make difficulty for you' (5:6). The passage then delves into the intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that wiping is wajib (obligatory in the terminological sense), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold that it is farḍ (obligatory in a stronger sense). The author discusses the distinction made by al-Razi: if the wound could be washed if exposed, then wiping is wajib/fard; if not, it is not obligatory. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is on the view of the two disciples (Sahibayn) as a precaution. However, some later authorities like al-Hidaya and al-Kanz prefer the view that wiping is mustahab, but al-Tahtawi clarifies that the correct view according to most is that wiping is wajib. The passage then lists conditions: the splint must be tied on the injury; it does not need to be tied in a state of purity (wudu); it is permissible to have a splint on one foot and wash the other; wiping is not invalidated if the splint falls off before healing. It also discusses cases of harm: if removing the bandage harms the wound, it should be left; if wiping directly harms, it is left. The author distinguishes between wiping as a substitute (badal) in ghusl and as an original (asl) in wudu, leading to different rulings when harm occurs. The final parts address special cases: if the entire head is wounded, there are two opinions on obligation; if someone cannot wipe or wash, tayammum or dropping the obligation may apply.

Key Terms
الجبيرة (al-jabirah): A splint made from sticks (e.g., palm branches) wrapped with paper or cloth, tied over a broken limb to stabilize it.
العصابة (al-‘isabah): A bandage or wrapping used to cover a wound, possibly including the splint.
الوضوء (al-wudu): Ritual washing prior to prayer; the passage discusses whether the splint must be tied in a state of purity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling on wiping over a splint (jabirah) in the Hanafi school?
A) It is mustahab (recommended) according to all views.
B) It is fard (obligatory) according to the Sahibayn, and fatwa is on that view as a precaution.
C) It is wajib but not fard, because the evidence is definitive.
D) It is not permissible at all.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a splint is an obligatory act in wudu (fard according to the mufta bihi), but its omission does not invalidate prayer under Abu Hanifa's view; the school's relied-upon position is precautionary.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Wiping Over Bandages (Jabirah): Rules and Distinctions
Details the Hanafi rules for wiping over bandages and similar wound coverings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping over bandages, distinguish it from wiping over khuffs as an original dispensation, and apply the condition of necessity.

Core Idea
Wiping over a splint is an original dispensation, not a time-limited substitute.

Wiping over bandages (jabirah) is a common necessity in daily life. The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on when and how to perform this wiping, treating it not as a substitute like wiping over khuffs, but as an original ruling due to necessity. In this lesson, we will unpack the key rules, conditions, and distinctions from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its trigger
Determine what act is being discussed: wiping over a bandage during wudu/ghusl. The trigger is necessity: removing the bandage would harm the wound.
If no harm, you must remove it and wash normally.
2
Contrast with khuffs (leather socks)
Wiping over khuffs is a pure substitute (badal mahd) with a time limit; wiping over jabirah is an original (asl) with no time limit. This affects rulings like permissibility of wiping when the bandage falls off.
Khuffs require intention? The text notes an ikhtilaf, but jabirah does not.
3
List the relaxations
Note what is not required: application in a state of purity, simultaneous washing of the other limb, and invalidation if the bandage falls off before healing.
These are concessions to remove hardship.
4
Identify additional scenarios
The text extends the ruling to eye inflammation, broken nails, and medicine. Same principle: if removal or washing harms, wiping is allowed.
The doctor's order or strong presumption justifies the dispensation.
5
Analyze the intention (niyyah) issue
The author clarifies that intention is not required for wiping over jabirah, khuffs (apparent view), or the head, because it is purification with water, unlike tayammum.
This is a critical refinement against those who require intention for khuffs.

Between the bandage of the moderate person and similar: if removing it harms him, it is untied due to necessity so that water does not seep into the wound. If removing does not harm, then untie and wash the healthy part and wipe the wounded part. If wiping harms, it is left. Wiping over the splint (jabirah) and similar is like washing what is under it, and it is not a substitute, unlike the khuff because it is a pure substitute. So wiping over the splint is not limited to a specific time because it is an original. The tying of the splint and similar in a state of purity is not a condition for the validity of wiping, to remove hardship. It is permissible to wipe one leg's splint while washing the other, because it is an original. The wiping does not become invalid by its falling off before healing, due to the excuse. Janabah and hadath are equal in this. It is permissible to wipe the upper bandage after wiping the lower, and the lower is not wiped after removing the upper. Its wiping does not become invalid by wetness under it, unlike the khuff. It is permissible to replace it with another after wiping it, and it is not obligatory to repeat the wiping on the replacement, but it is better to repeat on the second due to the semblance of substitution. If the eye becomes inflamed and a trustworthy Muslim doctor orders him not to wash it, or he strongly suspects harm from washing, he leaves it. If his fingernail breaks or he has an illness and places medicine or gum on it to prevent harm from water, or places a piece of gall bladder skin and its removal harms him, then wiping is permissible due to necessity. If wiping harms, he leaves it because necessity is measured by its extent. Intention is not required in wiping the khuff according to the apparent position, and some say it is required like tayammum due to substitution. Wiping the splint and wiping the head are equal in not requiring intention because it is purification with water.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah details the Hanafi rulings on wiping over bandages (jabirah) and similar coverings during wudu or ghusl. The author begins by stating that if removing the bandage would harm the wound, it is left on and one wipes over it; if no harm, it must be removed to wash the healthy area and wipe the wound. If wiping the wound itself causes harm, even wiping is omitted. This is based on the principle of necessity (darurah). The text then distinguishes wiping over a splint from wiping over leather socks (khuffs). Wiping over khuffs is a pure substitute limited to one day/night for residents and three for travelers, whereas wiping over a splint is not time-limited because it is treated as an original obligation due to necessity. Several conditions are relaxed: one is not required to have tied the splint in a state of purity; it is permissible to wipe one leg's splint while washing the other; the wiping is not invalidated if the splint falls off before healing. The commentary also covers scenarios like eye inflammation, broken nails, or applied medicine, where the same dispensation applies. Finally, the requirement of intention (niyyah) is discussed: for wiping over khuffs, the dominant view is that intention is not required, though some compare it to tayammum; for wiping over the splint and the head, there is consensus that intention is not required because these are purifications with water, which do not require intention like regular wudu. This passage is a critical refinement (hashiyah) that clarifies nuances and resolves potential objections.

Key Terms
جبيرة (jabirah): A splint or bandage applied to a wound or fracture.
عصابة (isabah): A bandage or wrap, especially for a wound.
مسح (mash): Wiping over a covering with a wet hand instead of washing.
بدل (badal): A substitute that replaces an original act, like wiping over khuffs replacing washing feet.
أصل (asl): Original act; here meaning the wiping is treated as the primary obligation under the circumstance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the validity of wudu if a bandage that was wiped falls off before the wound heals completely?
A) The wiping becomes invalid, and one must repeat it.
B) The wiping remains valid because the excuse still existed at the time of wiping.
C) It depends on whether the falling off was intentional.
D) The entire wudu is invalidated.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a bandage is a necessity-based original dispensation, not a substitute, so it is not time-limited and remains valid even if the bandage falls off before healing.

Introduction to Menstruation (Hayd) and Related Bloods
Opens the chapter on types of female blood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of blood that exit the female private part according to the Hanafi school and distinguish between them based on their basic definitions.

Core Idea
Three distinct bloods: hayd, nifas, and istihadah — each with its own rulings.

This opening segment establishes the foundational classification of female blood types. Understanding this taxonomy is essential because each type triggers different legal obligations and prohibitions. The hashiyah explains why menstruation is mentioned first: it is the most common occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject
The passage defines the types of blood that exit the female private part. Note that the author moves from common impurities to less common events.
2
Learn the three Arabic terms
Memorize: حيض (hayd), نفاس (nifas), استحاضة (istihadah). These are the three categories.
The order is deliberate: hayd is most frequent.
3
Understand the reason for the order
The hashiyah notes that after frequent events, the author addresses less frequent ones, and among these, hayd is the most common, so it is placed first.
This reflects a pedagogical principle: start with the more familiar.

Source Text

باب الحيض والنفاس والاستحاضة (يخرج من الفرج) أي بالمرور منه ثلاثة دماء (حيض ونفاس) ومقرهم .
لما ذكر الأحداث التي يكثر وقوعها ذكر أحداثاً يقل وقوعها، وقدم ذكر الحيض لأنه أكثر.
Chapter on menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and istihadah. (It emerges from the private part) i.e., three types of blood exit from it: menstrual, postpartum, and their [the women's] term? And the reason for mentioning: when he mentioned the frequent events, he then mentioned the less frequent events, and he gave precedence to menstruation because it is more common.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This brief passage marks the beginning of a new chapter on the types of blood that can exit the female private part. The matn (core text) defines three categories: hayd (menstruation), nifas (postpartum bleeding), and istihadah (irregular non-menstrual bleeding). The hashiyah (commentary) explains the author's arrangement: after treating the common impurities in previous chapters, the less common but significant events are now addressed, with menstruation placed first due to its frequency. This sets the stage for detailed rulings on each type.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood; a naturally occurring blood that exits the uterus at intervals.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding following childbirth.
استحاضة (istihadah): Irregular bleeding outside of the normal hayd or nifas period; often considered a chronic excuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies female bleeding into three types: hayd, nifas, and istihadah, each with distinct legal implications.

Wiping over Bandages and the Principle of Necessity (Darurah)
Commentary on wiping over bandages under necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which wiping over a bandage (jabirah) is permitted, apply the Hanafi maxim 'necessity is measured by its extent', and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether passing water over the medicine is required.

Core Idea
Wiping over bandages is a concession limited by necessity; the ruling is strictly bounded by the harm avoided.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi rulings on wiping over bandages (jabirah) during wudu when one has a medical condition such as a broken bone, wound, or inflammation. The passage from al-Tahtawi's hashiah clarifies that this is a concession based on necessity, and that the ruling is tailored to the extent of harm. We will also explore the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether one must pass water over the medicine or if mere wiping suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? Wiping over a bandage (jabirah) placed for medical treatment. What is the ruling? It is permitted (ja'iz) as a concession when necessity requires.
The permission applies only if removing the bandage or washing the wound would cause harm or delay healing.
2
Apply the maxim 'al-darurah tuqaddaru bi qadariha'
The scope of the concession is strictly limited by the extent of necessity. If removal of the bandage causes harm, wiping suffices; if wiping itself causes harm, even wiping is omitted.
This maxim governs all rukhsas in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Examine the conditions for valid mash
The conditions include: (a) the bandage covers a wound or break, (b) removing it would cause harm or delay healing, (c) the mash is performed during wudu on the affected limb. Intention is not required.
Unlike khuff, there is no fixed time limit for jabirah; it lasts as long as necessity.
4
Analyze the dispute on passing water
Some scholars (al-Halabi, al-Halwani) require passing water over the medicine; others say if removal harms, nothing is required. The mufta bihi view is not to require passing water if it causes harm.
The precaution (ihtiyat) is to pass water if possible.
5
Note the effect of healing and bandage removal
If healing occurs and the bandage remains on, the wiping remains valid only if removal would cause harm. If removal is harmless, the wiping becomes invalid.
This is due to the cessation of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العصابة العليا بعد مسح السفلى ولا يمسح السفلى بعد نزع العليا، ولا يبطل مسحها بابتلال ما تحتها بخلاف الخف ويجوز تبديلها بغيرها) بعد مسحها (ولا يجب إعادة المسح عليها) أي الموضوعة بدلاً (والأفضل إعادته على الثانية لشبهة البدلية (وإذا رمد وأمر) أي أمره طبيب مسلم حاذق أن لا يغسل عينه) أو غلب على ظنه ضرر الغسل تركه (أو انكسر ظفره) أو حصل به داء (وجعل عليه دواء أو علكا لمنع ضرر الماء ونحوه (أو) جعل عليه (جلدة مرارة) ونحوها وضره نزعه جاز له المسح للضرورة (وإن ضره المسح تركه) لأن الضرورة تقدر بقدرها (ولا يفتقر إلى النية في مسح (الخف في الأظهر وقيل تشترط فيه كالتيمم للبدلية (و) مسح (الجبيرة و) مسح (الرأس) فهي سواء في عدم اشتراط النية لأنه طهارة بالماء. وجد البرء ولم تسقط ذكر الكرابيسي أن المسح يبطل قال في النهر : وينبغي أن يقيد بما إذا لم يضره إزالة الجبيرة أما إذا ضره لشدة الصوقها، فلا وإذا سقطت عن برء في الصلاة قبل القعود قدر التشهد أفسدت، وبعده تكون من الإثني عشرية قوله : (ولا يمسح السفلى بعد نزع العليا) أي لا يطالب بمسحها، بل يكفي عنه مسح العليا. قوله : (بخلاف الخف أي في المسائل الثماينة أربعة في المتن وأربعة في الشرح قوله : (ولا يجب إعادة المسح عليها لأنه كالغسل لما تحتها وقد سقط بالمسح الأول كما إذا مسح رأسه ثم حلقه . قوله : ( وإذا رمد) بكسر العين أي هاجت عينه . قوله : (أو جعل عليه جلدة مرارة) ولو جاوزت موضع القرحة كما في الخانية. قوله : (جاز له المسح مثله في البناية، والفتح والبرهان وذكر الحلبي أنه يجب عليه إمرار الماء، ولا يكفيه المسح لعدم الضرورة قال في المخ وهو المصرح به في عامة الكتب المعتمدة، وجرى عليه في الدرر، وفي الشرنبلالية عن التتارخانية معزياً إلى الأصل أنه إذا ضره نزع الدواء لا يشترط المسح ولا إمرار الماء على الدواء من غير ذكر خلاف ثم قال وشرط شمس الأئمة الحلواني إمرار الماء على الدواء، ولا يكفيه المسح اهـ قال بعض الأفاضل والظاهر أن فيه إختلافاً والإشتراط فيه إحتياط. قوله : ( ومسح الجبيرة ومسح الرأس) عدم النية فيهما متفق عليه. قوله: (لأنه طهارة بالماء) أي فلا يفتقر إلى النية كالوضوء، ولأنه بعض الوضوء.
The upper bandage after wiping the lower, and one does not wipe the lower after removing the upper; its wiping is not invalidated by wetness underneath, unlike the khuff. It is permissible to replace it with another after wiping it, and re-wiping on it is not required, i.e., on the replacement. However, it is better to re-wipe on the second due to the semblance of substitution. If one has ophthalmia and is ordered by a skilled Muslim physician not to wash his eye, or one believes washing will harm him, he leaves it. Or if his nail breaks or a disease occurs and he puts medicine or gum on it to prevent harm from water and the like, or he puts a piece of gall bladder skin and the like, and its removal would harm him, then wiping over it is permitted due to necessity. If wiping harms him, he leaves it, because necessity is measured by its extent. The most apparent view is that intention is not required for wiping the khuff, though it is said that it is required like tayammum due to substitution. Similarly, wiping the jabirah and the head are the same in that intention is not required, because it is purification with water. If healing occurs and the bandage does not fall, al-Karabisi transmitted that the wiping becomes invalid. In al-Nahr it is said: It should be qualified by when removing the bandage does not harm him; if it does harm due to strong adhesion, then not. If it falls off after healing during prayer before sitting for the tashahhud length, it invalidates; after that, it becomes from the Twelve. He said: (And he does not wipe the lower after removing the upper) meaning he is not required to wipe it; rather wiping the upper suffices. He said: (Unlike the khuff) regarding eight matters: four in the text and four in the commentary. He said: (And re-wiping on it is not required) because it is like washing what is underneath, and it has been fulfilled by the first wiping, just as if one wiped his head then shaved it. He said: (If one has ophthalmia) meaning his eye became inflamed. He said: (Or he puts a piece of gall bladder skin) even if it exceeds the wound site as in al-Khaniyya. He said: (He is permitted to wipe) similarly in al-Binaya, al-Fath, and al-Burhan. Al-Halabi mentioned that he must pass water over it, and wiping does not suffice due to lack of necessity. In al-Muhit it is stated that this is the explicit view in most reliable books, and it is followed in al-Durar. In al-Shurunbulaliyya from al-TatarKhaniyya, attributed to the original, that if removing the medicine harms him, neither wiping nor passing water over the medicine is required, without mention of dissent. Then he said: Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani stipulated passing water over the medicine, and wiping does not suffice. Some scholars said: The apparent is that there is disagreement, and stipulating is precautionary. He said: (And wiping the jabirah and wiping the head) that intention is not required in both is agreed upon. He said: (Because it is purification with water) so it does not require intention like wudu, and because it is part of wudu.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of rulings on wiping over bandages and medical necessities, from al-Tahtawi's hashiah on Maraqi al-Falah. It begins by stating that when one has an upper bandage over a lower one, after wiping the lower, one does not need to wipe the lower again when removing the upper; wiping the upper suffices. If the bandage gets wet underneath, the wiping is not invalidated, unlike the case of khuff. It is permissible to replace a bandage after wiping it, and re-wiping on the replacement is not obligatory, though it is better to re-wipe due to the semblance of substitution. The passage then addresses medical conditions: if one has ophthalmia (ramad) and a Muslim physician advises not to wash the eye, or one believes washing would cause harm, he may leave washing and wipe instead. Similarly, if a nail breaks and medicine or gum is applied to prevent water damage, or a piece of gall bladder skin is placed and removal would cause harm, wiping over it is permitted due to necessity. If wiping itself would cause harm, it is also left, as necessity is measured by its extent. On the issue of intention (niyyah) for wiping, the relied-upon view (al-azhar) is that intention is not required for wiping the khuff, the jabirah, or the head, because these are purifications with water, not with earth (like tayammum). However, there is a view that intention is required for khuff by analogy with tayammum due to substitution. The commentary then addresses the case where the wound heals but the bandage does not fall off: al-Karabisi transmitted that the wiping becomes invalid. But al-Tahtawi qualifies this: it is invalid only if removing the bandage does not cause harm; if it does cause harm, the wiping remains valid. If the bandage falls off after healing during prayer before sitting for tashahhud, the prayer is invalidated; if after, it is considered from the Twelve (a technical term). The commentary also clarifies that the ruling of not needing to re-wipe on a new bandage is analogous to washing underneath, and the first wiping suffices, similar to wiping the head then shaving it. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the necessity of passing water over the medicine: al-Halabi and al-Halwani hold that passing water is required and mere wiping does not suffice in the absence of harm, while others, from the original sources, hold that if removal harms, neither wiping nor passing water is required. Al-Tahtawi notes that the precaution is to require passing water. Finally, it is confirmed that the agreement on no intention for jabirah and head is unanimous.

Key Terms
جبيرة (jabirah): A splint or bandage placed over a wound or broken bone for treatment.
مسح (mash): Wiping with wet hand over a body part or covering (like khuff, bandage) as part of wudu.
الضرورة (al-darurah): A state of necessity that permits normally invalid acts to avoid harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the wiping over a bandage become invalid according to the relied-upon view?
A) When the wound heals and the bandage remains on.
B) When the wound heals and removal of the bandage does not cause harm.
C) When the bandage gets wet.
D) When the bandage is replaced with a new one.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over bandages is a necessity-based concession; its scope is strictly limited by the harm avoided, and the mufta bihi view does not require intention or passing water when removal would cause harm.

The Necessity of Intention (Niyyah) for Wiping over Khuff, Jabirah, and Head
Commentary on the necessity of intention for wiping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that intention is not required for wiping (mash) over khuff, jabirah, and head, analyze the qiyas argument that would require intention, and identify the practical consequences of the disagreement.

Core Idea
Intention is not required for mash because it is purification with water; the analogy with tayammum fails due to a difference in the purifying medium.

This lesson focuses on the dispute regarding the necessity of intention (niyyah) for the act of wiping (mash) over khuff (leather socks), jabirah (bandages), and the head during wudu. The Hanafi relied-upon view holds that intention is not required, whereas some later Hanafis and the Shafi'i school require it. We will examine the reasoning behind each position, particularly the qiyas with tayammum, and understand why the Hanafi masters rejected the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Is intention (niyyah) required for the validity of mash over khuff, jabirah, and head?
This is a sub-issue within the broader topic of conditions of wudu.
2
State the Hanafi position
The relied-upon view (al-azhar) is that intention is not required. This is because mash is a purification with water (taharah bil-ma'), and the regular wudu actions (washing face, arms, etc.) do not require intention; mash is part of wudu.
The same applies to jabirah and head.
3
Present the opposing argument
Some scholars (minority view within Hanafi and the Shafi'i school) argue that intention is required because mash is a substitute (badal) for washing the actual body part, similar to tayammum which substitutes wudu and requires intention.
The opposing view uses qiyas (analogy) with tayammum.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The analogy with tayammum is invalid because tayammum is a complete alternative purification with earth (taharah bil-turab), whereas mash is a partial act within wudu and uses water. The ruling on intention depends on the medium: water purifies without specific intention, while earth requires intention because it is a substitute.
Hadith: 'Actions are by intentions' applies to the overall act of wudu, not each part.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
If one wipes without intending, the mash is valid according to the Hanafi mufta bihi; if intention were required, the wudu would be incomplete and would need repetition.
This difference has practical implications in daily worship.

The upper bandage after wiping the lower, and one does not wipe the lower after removing the upper; its wiping is not invalidated by wetness underneath, unlike the khuff. It is permissible to replace it with another after wiping it, and re-wiping on it is not required, i.e., on the replacement. However, it is better to re-wipe on the second due to the semblance of substitution. If one has ophthalmia and is ordered by a skilled Muslim physician not to wash his eye, or one believes washing will harm him, he leaves it. Or if his nail breaks or a disease occurs and he puts medicine or gum on it to prevent harm from water and the like, or he puts a piece of gall bladder skin and the like, and its removal would harm him, then wiping over it is permitted due to necessity. If wiping harms him, he leaves it, because necessity is measured by its extent. The most apparent view is that intention is not required for wiping the khuff, though it is said that it is required like tayammum due to substitution. Similarly, wiping the jabirah and the head are the same in that intention is not required, because it is purification with water. If healing occurs and the bandage does not fall, al-Karabisi transmitted that the wiping becomes invalid. In al-Nahr it is said: It should be qualified by when removing the bandage does not harm him; if it does harm due to strong adhesion, then not. If it falls off after healing during prayer before sitting for the tashahhud length, it invalidates; after that, it becomes from the Twelve. He said: (And he does not wipe the lower after removing the upper) meaning he is not required to wipe it; rather wiping the upper suffices. He said: (Unlike the khuff) regarding eight matters: four in the text and four in the commentary. He said: (And re-wiping on it is not required) because it is like washing what is underneath, and it has been fulfilled by the first wiping, just as if one wiped his head then shaved it. He said: (If one has ophthalmia) meaning his eye became inflamed. He said: (Or he puts a piece of gall bladder skin) even if it exceeds the wound site as in al-Khaniyya. He said: (He is permitted to wipe) similarly in al-Binaya, al-Fath, and al-Burhan. Al-Halabi mentioned that he must pass water over it, and wiping does not suffice due to lack of necessity. In al-Muhit it is stated that this is the explicit view in most reliable books, and it is followed in al-Durar. In al-Shurunbulaliyya from al-TatarKhaniyya, attributed to the original, that if removing the medicine harms him, neither wiping nor passing water over the medicine is required, without mention of dissent. Then he said: Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani stipulated passing water over the medicine, and wiping does not suffice. Some scholars said: The apparent is that there is disagreement, and stipulating is precautionary. He said: (And wiping the jabirah and wiping the head) that intention is not required in both is agreed upon. He said: (Because it is purification with water) so it does not require intention like wudu, and because it is part of wudu.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of rulings on wiping over bandages and medical necessities, from al-Tahtawi's hashiah on Maraqi al-Falah. It begins by stating that when one has an upper bandage over a lower one, after wiping the lower, one does not need to wipe the lower again when removing the upper; wiping the upper suffices. If the bandage gets wet underneath, the wiping is not invalidated, unlike the case of khuff. It is permissible to replace a bandage after wiping it, and re-wiping on the replacement is not obligatory, though it is better to re-wipe due to the semblance of substitution. The passage then addresses medical conditions: if one has ophthalmia (ramad) and a Muslim physician advises not to wash the eye, or one believes washing would cause harm, he may leave washing and wipe instead. Similarly, if a nail breaks and medicine or gum is applied to prevent water damage, or a piece of gall bladder skin is placed and removal would cause harm, wiping over it is permitted due to necessity. If wiping itself would cause harm, it is also left, as necessity is measured by its extent. On the issue of intention (niyyah) for wiping, the relied-upon view (al-azhar) is that intention is not required for wiping the khuff, the jabirah, or the head, because these are purifications with water, not with earth (like tayammum). However, there is a view that intention is required for khuff by analogy with tayammum due to substitution. The commentary then addresses the case where the wound heals but the bandage does not fall off: al-Karabisi transmitted that the wiping becomes invalid. But al-Tahtawi qualifies this: it is invalid only if removing the bandage does not cause harm; if it does cause harm, the wiping remains valid. If the bandage falls off after healing during prayer before sitting for tashahhud, the prayer is invalidated; if after, it is considered from the Twelve (a technical term). The commentary also clarifies that the ruling of not needing to re-wipe on a new bandage is analogous to washing underneath, and the first wiping suffices, similar to wiping the head then shaving it. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the necessity of passing water over the medicine: al-Halabi and al-Halwani hold that passing water is required and mere wiping does not suffice in the absence of harm, while others, from the original sources, hold that if removal harms, neither wiping nor passing water is required. Al-Tahtawi notes that the precaution is to require passing water. Finally, it is confirmed that the agreement on no intention for jabirah and head is unanimous.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention; a condition for acts of worship that distinguishes them from habits or identifies which act is being performed.
بدلية (badaliyyah): Substitution; the quality of being a substitute for another obligation.
الأظهر (al-azhar): The most apparent or relied-upon view in the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given in the passage for not requiring intention when wiping the head, khuff, and jabirah?
A) Because these are sunnah acts.
B) Because they are purification with water and part of wudu.
C) Because they are substitutes (badal) for washing.
D) Because the hadith 'Actions by intentions' applies only to prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not require intention for wiping over khuff, jabirah, or head because these are purifications with water, not substitute purifications; the qiyas with tayammum is rejected due to the difference in the purifying medium.

Taharah — Purification

Defining Menstruation (Hayd): Nature and Rulings in Hanafi Fiqh
Opening the chapter defining key blood types and their significance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hayd (menstruation), distinguish it from istihadah, and identify the key rulings that depend on its classification as hadath.

Core Idea
Menstruation in Hanafi fiqh is a ritual impurity (hadath), not a physical impurity (najas), shaping all its associated rulings.

Menstruation is a natural occurrence but carries profound legal consequences. In this lesson, we will explore the Hanafi definition of hayd, its nature as hadath rather than najas, and the many rulings—from prayer to divorce—that hinge on its correct identification. We'll also touch on the minimum age and the distinction between hayd and other bleeding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three blood types
The passage states that three types of blood exit the vulva: hayd (menstruation), nifas (postpartum), and istihadah (non-menstrual). Note their different sources: hayd and nifas from the uterus, istihadah from a vein.
Istihadah is a chronic condition, not a ritual impurity.
2
Understand the legal definition of hayd
Hayd is defined as blood expelled forcefully from the uterus of a woman who has reached nine years of age and is free from disease. This definition excludes pathological bleeding and establishes a minimum age.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) sets the minimum age at nine lunar years.
3
Recognize hayd as hadath, not najas
The text argues that hayd is a hadath (ritual impurity) because if it were najas, washing during menses would permit prayer, but it does not. Purification from hayd is like purification from minor hadath, requiring ghusl, not just removal of impurity.
This distinction affects rulings on touching the Quran, entering mosques, etc.
4
Survey the rulings affected by hayd
The passage lists many rulings: divorce (bid'i vs sunni), manumission (iddah for umm walad), istibra, iddah (three menses for free woman, two for slave), lineage (child born after six months of separation), intercourse (prohibited), prayer, fasting, Quran recitation, touching the Quran, i'tikaf, entering mosque, tawaf, and determining puberty.
These rulings show why hayd is 'among the greatest important matters.'
5
Note the ikhtilaf on age and on kufr
There is a minority view that hayd can occur between ages 5 and 9, but the fatwa is on 9. Also, some scholars deemed believing intercourse during menses permissible as kufr, but the verified view is that it is not kufr because the prohibition is due to harm (ghayr li-aynihi).
This nuance affects how we judge a person’s faith.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والطهارة منه طهارة حدث لا طهارة نجس، ولأن الأحكام المتعلقة به من حرمة القراءة، ونحوها هي الأحكام المختصة بالأحداث وسببه الإبتدائي ما قيل أن أمنا حواء لما كسرت شجرة الحنطة وأدمتها قال الله تعالى: لأدمينك كما أدميتها وابتلاها بالحيض هي وجميع بناتها إلى الساعة اهـ وأصابها بعد أن أهبطت من الجنة قوله : (أي بالمرور منه أشار به إلى أن الفرج لم يكن مقراً لهذه الدماء وإنما أضيفت إليه بإعتبار المرور منه لأنه الحيض والنفاس مقرهما الرحم والاستحاضة دم عرق قوله : (الأحكام كثيرة) علة لكونه من أعظم المهمات قوله : (كالطلاق) وجه الاحتياج إليه فيه إنه إن أوقعه فيه كان بدعياً وفي طهر بعده لا وطء فيه سنى. قوله : (والعتاق) فإن أم الولد إذا عتقت تعتد بعده بثلاث حيض. قوله: والاستبراء) فتستبرىء الحائض بحيضة . قوله : (والعدة) لذات الحيض فإنها للحرة ثلاث حيض وللأمة ثنتان قوله : ( والنسب) فإنها إذا طلقت واعتدت بثلاث حيض، ثم أتت بولد بعدها لستة أشهر لا يلحق وإن لم ترد ما يلحق إلى السنتين قوله : ( وحل الوطء) إذا طهرت منه وله أن يصدقها في حيضها وطهرها فيمتنع عنها في الأول ويقربها في الثاني ومن اعتقد حل Chapter on Menstruation, Postpartum Bleeding, and Non-Menstrual Uterine Bleeding. (It emerges from the vulva) meaning three bloods pass through it: (menstruation, postpartum bleeding) and their source [is the uterus] and non-menstrual bleeding. And he interpreted it by his saying: (Menstruation) is among the obscure chapters and the greatest of important matters due to many rulings, such as divorce, manumission, istibra, waiting period (iddah), lineage, permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, recitation of Quran, touching it, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, circumambulation of Hajj, and puberty. Its reality is: (blood that the uterus expels) i.e., forcefully pushes out, (the uterus) which is the place of raising the child from sperm, (a mature woman) of nine years, (without a disease) that would cause its occurrence. And no one should say that menstruation is a kind of impurity, for we say that removal of impurity permits entering prayer, but the menstruating woman's bathing does not permit that as long as she is characterized. So it is known by this that it is not true impurity, and purification from it is purification from hadath, not from impurity. And because the rulings related to it, such as prohibition of recitation etc., are rulings specific to hadath. Its initial cause is what was said: that our mother Eve broke the wheat tree and bloodied it, so Allah said: I will certainly blood you as you bloodied it, and afflicted her and all her daughters with menstruation until the Hour. That began after she descended from Paradise. He said: (Meaning by its passage) he indicated that the vulva is not the source of these bloods, but they are attributed to it by way of passage; because menstruation and postpartum blood originate in the uterus, and istihadah is blood from a vein. He said: (Many rulings) is the cause for it being among the greatest important matters. He said: (Such as divorce) the need for it is that if he divorces during it, it is bid'i; if in a subsequent pure period without intercourse, it is sunni. He said: (And manumission) for if a slave-girl who has borne a child (umm walad) is freed, she observes her iddah by three menstrual cycles. He said: (And istibra) the menstruating woman undergoes istibra with one menses. He said: (And iddah) for a woman with menses, for a free woman it is three menses, for a slave woman two. He said: (And lineage) if she is divorced and observes three menses, then gives birth after six months, the child is not attributed; but if she does not menstruate, it may be attributed up to two years. He said: (And permissibility of intercourse) when she becomes pure from it; he may believe her regarding her menses and purity, and he refrains in the former and approaches in the latter. Whoever believes intercourse during menses is permissible commits kufr, as stated in al-Mabsut, al-Ikhtiyar, and al-Fath. However, the author of al-Khulasah verified that it is not kufr. And in the words of the second chapter of kufr: Whoever believes something permissible is forbidden, or vice versa, commits kufr if the prohibition is inherent and established by definitive proof; if it is prohibition due to something else by definitive proof, or inherent by ahad hadith, he does not commit kufr. Therefore, one should not issue a fatwa that a person believing it permissible commits kufr, because the prohibition is due to something else (harm). He said: (And prayer and fasting) she does not perform them during menses, and performs them afterward. So if she does not know, she might leave them at their obligatory time and perform them at the time of required abandonment, both of which are forbidden and harmful. He said: (And touching it) minor hadath shares with menses in this. He said: (And circumambulation of Hajj) similarly minor hadath shares with it, though the expiation for sexual intercourse differs. He said: (And its reality is blood etc.) this is based on it being a kind of impurity; but the verified view is that it is from hadath, so it is defined as a legal impediment that extends for a known period, a minimum of three days and nights. He said: (From sperm) to explain reality. He said: (A mature woman of nine years) this is the fatwa; it is also said she may menstruate between five and nine; but a girl of five does not menstruate by consensus.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Hayd (menstruation), Nifas (postpartum bleeding), and Istihadah (non-menstrual uterine bleeding). It is an expansion probe by al-Tahtawi on the matn of Maraqi al-Falah. The section begins by stating that three types of blood exit the vulva: hayd, nifas, and istihadah. The author notes that hayd is one of the most subtle and important topics due to its many associated rulings, including divorce, manumission, istibra (waiting period to ensure non-pregnancy), iddah (waiting period after divorce), lineage, permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, Quran recitation, touching the Quran, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, tawaf of Hajj, and the determination of puberty. The legal definition (haqiqah) of hayd is given: blood expelled forcefully from the uterus of a woman who has reached the age of nine and is free from a disease that causes bleeding. The author then clarifies that hayd is not true impurity (najas) but rather a state of hadath (ritual impurity). This is because if it were najas, bathing during menstruation would permit entering prayer, but it does not; rather, purification from hayd is like purification from hadath. The rulings associated with hayd, such as prohibition of Quran recitation and touching, are similar to those of minor hadath (i.e., needing wudu). The passage also recounts a story that the initial cause of menstruation was Eve's transgression with the wheat tree. The hashiah then comments on each phrase: 'from the vulva' indicates passage, not origin; the source of hayd and nifas is the uterus, while istihadah is from a vein. The many rulings are detailed: divorce during menses is bid'i whereas in a pure period without intercourse is sunni; the iddah for a freed umm walad is three menses; istibra is one menses; a free woman's iddah is three menses, a slave's two; lineage is established if birth occurs after a minimum of six months from separation; intercourse is prohibited during menses, and believing it permissible is a grave matter (but not necessarily kufr if the prohibition is due to something else). Prayer and fasting are prohibited during menses, and she must make up the fasts but not the prayers. Touching the Quran and tawaf share the same ruling with minor hadath. Finally, the definition of hayd is clarified: based on impurity views it would be different, but the verified Hanafi view is that it is a legal impediment (mani' shar'i) extending for a minimum of three days and nights. The minimum age for menstruation is nine years by fatwa, though some said between five and nine; a girl under five never menstruates by consensus.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Blood expelled from the uterus of a healthy woman who has reached puberty (9 years), as a natural phenomenon, not due to disease or childbirth.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding that occurs after childbirth.
استحاضة (istihadah): Non-menstrual uterine bleeding from a vein (irqi), which does not prevent prayer or fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi), what is the minimum age for a girl's menstruation?
A) 9 years
B) 5 years
C) 12 years
D) 7 years

Key Takeaway
Menstruation is a hadath (ritual impurity) with a minimum age of nine and duration of three days, affecting many rulings including prayer, fasting, and intercourse.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Definition and Rulings of Menstruation (Hayd)
Defines hayd and lists its rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hayd, distinguish it as a state of hadath (not najis), and apply its minimum/maximum durations and conditions to determine whether blood counts as hayd.

Core Idea
Menstruation is a state of ritual impurity (hadath), not physical impurity (najis), governing numerous worship and family rulings.

This passage from al-Tahtawi’s Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah defines hayd and clarifies its legal nature. Understanding hayd is critical because it affects prayer, fasting, Quran recitation, divorce, waiting periods, and more. We will explore the definition, conditions, duration, and key distinctions that make hayd a state of hadath rather than an impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Hayd is not an act but a natural condition with legal consequences. The passage lists twelve rulings affected (e.g., divorce, prayer, fasting). Recognize that hayd is classified as a state of hadath (ritual impurity), not a physical impurity (najis).
2
Deconstruct the definition components
The legal definition: blood expelled forcefully from the womb of a mature female (at least nine years old) who has no illness causing the bleeding and is not pregnant. Identify each component critically.
3
Distinguish hadath from najis
The author argues hayd is hadath because its rulings (e.g., prohibition of prayer, recitation) match those of other hadath states, and purification is via ghusl (for hadath), not mere washing. This distinction has practical effects: a woman in hayd may touch the Quran with a barrier, etc.
4
Determine conditions and durations
Minimum: three whole days and nights (72 hours). Average: five days. Maximum: ten days (the relied-upon view; some say fifteen). Age: from nine to fifty-five years. Blood must be from the womb, not due to disease or pregnancy. If blood appears on the first and last day, even with a clean interval, the whole period is hayd.
5
Apply to a scenario
A woman sees dark, thick blood for two days then stops. Is this hayd? No, because it is less than three days. Apply the same reasoning to: a girl aged eight sees blood; a woman aged sixty sees blood; a pregnant woman bleeds. Determine whether each is hayd based on the conditions.

(And istihadah) and he explained it by saying: (Al-hayd) is among the obscure chapters and the greatest of important matters due to many rulings, such as divorce, manumission, istibra', waiting period, lineage, permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, reciting Quran and touching it, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, tawaf of Hajj, and puberty. Its reality: (Blood that a womb expels) i.e., forcefully pushes (a womb), which is the place of raising the child from a drop, (mature) nine years old, (without a disease that would cause) the occurrence (of blood due to it). No one should say that menses is of the category of impurities, for we say: removing impurity permits entering prayer, and the woman in menses bathes as long as she is described as such... And the purification from it is purification from a minor impurity (hadath), not from an impurity (najis). Because the rulings related to it, such as the prohibition of recitation etc., are the rulings specific to states of impurity... Its linguistic origin is 'flowing'; it is said 'the valley menstruated' when it flowed. (The minimum of hayd is three days and their nights). These are its conditions. Its pillar is the emergence of the specific blood. Its description: blood closer to blackness, [unclear: thick?], foul-smelling. (Its average is five days and its maximum is ten) with their nights, according to the text on its count... And (nifas) linguistically is the verbal noun of nafisat...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and explanation of hayd (menstruation) from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah. The author begins by affirming that hayd is one of the most complex and important topics in fiqh due to the many rulings that depend on it. He lists twelve areas where hayd is relevant: divorce, manumission, istibra', 'iddah, lineage, permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, recitation of Quran, touching the Quran, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, tawaf of Hajj, and determination of puberty. This shows how central the correct identification of hayd is to Islamic law. The author then gives the legal definition: blood expelled forcefully from the womb of a mature woman who has reached nine years of age, not due to an illness that would cause bleeding, and not during pregnancy. The text clarifies that hayd is not a physical impurity (najis) but a state of ritual impurity (hadath). The evidence for this is that the rulings attached to hayd (like prohibition of prayer, fasting, Quran recitation) are the same as those for other hadath states, and the woman performs ghusl to become pure, which is the purification for hadath, not for removing najis. The author also mentions the story of Hawwa (Eve) as the origin of hayd. Then the passage discusses the linguistic meaning of hayd, tracing it to the root meaning of 'flowing'. It gives examples from Arabic usage. The author then presents the conditions and duration: minimum three days and nights (72 hours), average five days, maximum ten days (according to the relied-upon view, though some say fifteen). The blood must be from the womb, not due to illness, and the woman must be between nine and fifty-five years old. The pillar (rukn) is the emergence of blood that has the specific description: dark, thick, foul-smelling. The six colors that are considered hayd are black, red, yellow, dull brown, green, and 'turbid' (unclear). The author notes that the blood need not flow continuously; even a small amount on the first and last day suffices, and any interval of purity within the duration is included. The passage ends with a brief mention of nifas (postpartum bleeding), noting its linguistic derivation. The commentary emphasizes the practical implications: a woman must know her hayd days to properly fulfill religious obligations like prayer, fasting, and to correctly compute her 'iddah after divorce.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): Blood expelled forcefully from the womb of a mature female, not due to illness or pregnancy, with a minimum duration of three days.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Bleeding from a vein (irq) that comes during non-menstrual time, not from the womb.
النفاس (al-nifas): Postpartum bleeding, linguistically derived from 'nafisat' (to give birth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum number of days and nights for hayd according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) One day and one night
B) Three days and three nights
C) Five days
D) Ten days

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh defines hayd as uterine bleeding lasting at least three days (72 hours), occurring in women aged 9–55, and treating it as a state of hadath (ritual impurity) rather than physical impurity, with specific rulings on worship and family law.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Hanafi Definition of Hayd: Duration, Conditions, and Characteristics
Defines hayd legally and linguistically, states its duration and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of hayd, identify its minimum, average, and maximum durations, list its conditions, and describe the characteristic blood that distinguishes it from istihadah.

Core Idea
Hayd is defined by a minimum of three days, a maximum of ten, specific blood characteristics, and conditions of age, health, and absence of pregnancy.

This passage from Al-Tahtawi's Hashiya lays out the foundational parameters of hayd in the Hanafi school. Understanding these boundaries is essential for distinguishing menstrual blood from irregular bleeding (istihadah) and for correctly applying rulings related to prayer, fasting, and marital relations. The text carefully separates the linguistic and legal definitions and highlights the relied-upon opinion on duration.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic vs. legal definition
Hayd linguistically means 'flowing', as in a valley flowing or a tree exuding gum. Legally, it is blood that exits from the womb of a woman due to health (not disease), while she is not pregnant and has not reached menopause (55 years). This distinction helps understand the scope of the term.
The legal definition narrows the general meaning to specific conditions.
2
Identify the three temporal parameters
The minimum is three days with their nights (72 hours), the average is five days, and the maximum is ten days with their nights. The text notes that continuity of flow is not required; it suffices to see blood at the beginning and end of the period, even if it stops in between.
The maximum of ten is the mufta bihi opinion; a minority view of fifteen is mentioned but not relied upon.
3
List the conditions of hayd
The blood must: (1) come from the womb; (2) be from a woman of menstruating age (capable of bearing children); (3) not be caused by illness or infection; (4) not occur during pregnancy; (5) be preceded by a period of purity (at least 15 days of istihadah or tuhr); (6) be one of six colors: black, red, yellow, dull, green, or earthy.
The pillar (rukn) is the emergence of blood beyond the place of virginity (the external vulva).
4
Characterize the blood of hayd
Hayd blood is described as blackish (inclined to black), sharp/tangy (like something burning on the tongue), and foul-smelling. These features distinguish it from istihadah blood, which lacks these qualities.
The sharpness (ladhāgh) and smell are key discriminators.
5
Recognize the authority of the ruling
The ten-day maximum is based on textual evidence (hadith). The commentary notes that the condition of continuity is not required; the entire period between the first and last sighting counts as hayd even with gaps.
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah; the minority opinion of fifteen is considered weak.

Book of Purification: Blood exits due to it [hayd] and there is no pregnancy because Allah Almighty has established His custom that the mouth of the womb closes with pregnancy, so nothing exits from it until the child or most of it comes out, and she has not reached the age of menopause which is fifty-five years according to the relied-upon opinion. This is its definition legally. As for linguistically, its origin is flowing. It is said 'the valley hayada' when it flowed. (The minimum of hayd is three days with their nights, and these are its conditions and its pillar is the emergence of specific blood, and its description is blood inclined to blackness, sharp, with a bad smell. (Its average is five days and its maximum is ten) with their nights due to the text regarding its number, and it was said fifteen days. Continuity is not a condition, so its cessation within its duration is like its continuity. And (nifas) linguistically is the verbal noun of nafasat. [Then the commentary begins:] His statement: (requires blood exiting due to it) indicates that the meaning is not absolute disease, for sickness of a healthy womb does not prevent hayd. His statement: (As for linguistically, its origin is (flowing) it would have been better to mention the linguistic meaning first according to the custom of authors. His statement: (it is said 'the valley hayada' when it flowed) And it is said: the tree hayadat when red gum came out of it, and the hare hayadat when blood came out of its womb, and the woman hayadat so she is ha'id (menstruating) without the tā' in classical Arabic because it is a necessary description for the feminine so no confusion. Al-Farrā' narrated ha'idah. And in al-Qamus, it is said from it is 'al-hawd' (the basin) because water flows to it. And some collected the animals that menstruate, ten in number, by his saying: Menstruation comes to women and nine [others]: the she-camel, the hyena, the hare, the gecko, the bat, the stone marten, the bitch, the weasel, and the snakes are counted. Some added 'samīkah' (?) and 'ra'āshah' (?) so memorize, for in memorizing analogous things one desires. And menstruation attributed to these animals means flowing. His statement: (The minimum of hayd) means the time of its minimum so that the statement is correct. His statement: (with their nights) the annexation is not for specification, so it is not necessary that the nights be the nights of those days, as in Majma' al-Anhar. The criterion is seventy-two hours as in al-Qahistani, and this is the apparent narration. Know that it is not a condition that the blood flow continuously for three or ten because that is rare; seeing it every day, even a little, is sufficient as in al-Siraj. Rather, what is considered is its presence at the beginning and end of the period, even if a period of purity intervenes between them, and the whole is considered hayd. His statement: (And these are its conditions) means what was mentioned earlier: that it be from the womb of a woman of age capable of bearing children, no pregnancy, and there remains that a period of purity precedes it. His statement: (And its pillar is the emergence of specific blood) is an annexation of what is a description, i.e., the blood that appears. As for emergence, its condition is establishment, and it is the six colors: black, red, yellow, dull, green, and earthy. Its time of establishment is by emergence, which is known by passing the place of virginity, which is by exiting to the apparent vulva, analogously to nullifiers of wudu. Ihtisha' (inserting cotton) is sunnah for a non-virgin, and recommended for a virgin during hayd. As for during purity, it is recommended for a non-virgin but not for a virgin. His statement: (And its description is blood inclined to blackness) this is according to its prevailing state, so it does not negate the previous colors. His statement: (sharp) with dhāl and ghayn.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage from Al-Tahtawi's Hashiya on Maraqi al-Falah defines hayd (menstruation) and provides its legal rulings. The author first distinguishes the legal definition from the linguistic one. Legally, hayd is blood that exits from the womb due to health (not disease) in a woman who is not pregnant and has not reached menopause (55 years). Linguistically, it means flowing. The minimum duration is three complete days (72 hours), the average is five, and the maximum is ten. An alternative opinion of fifteen is mentioned but not relied upon. The text clarifies that continuity of flow is not required; seeing blood at the beginning and end of the period suffices, even if it stops in between. The conditions include the woman being of menstruating age, not pregnant, and the blood being of specific colors (six colors listed). The pillar (rukn) is the emergence of blood. The description of hayd blood is that it is blackish, sharp (burning on the tongue), and has a bad smell, which distinguishes it from istihadah (chronic bleeding) which has no smell. The commentary also discusses the linguistic aspects and the animals that menstruate metaphorically. The relied-upon opinion (mufta bihi) is that the maximum is ten days.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Blood that exits from the womb of a woman due to health (not disease) during the period of fertility, not pregnancy, and before menopause.
أقل الحيض (aqall al-hayd): The minimum duration of hayd, which is three days and their nights (72 hours).
أكثر الحيض (akthar al-hayd): The maximum duration of hayd, which is ten days and their nights.
استحاضة (istihadah): Chronic or irregular vaginal bleeding that is not hayd, characterized by not meeting the conditions of hayd (e.g., less than three days or more than ten, or without the characteristic smell).
لذاغ (ladhāgh): A description of hayd blood meaning 'sharp' or 'burning' (like a sharp taste on the tongue).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum duration of hayd?
A) 3 days
B) 10 days
C) 15 days
D) 40 days

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hayd hinges on specific temporal limits (3–10 days), blood characteristics, and conditions related to age, pregnancy, and health; continuity of flow is not required.

Definition and Duration of Nifas (Postpartum Bleeding) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines nifas legally, states its maximum duration, and discusses related rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of nifas, identify its maximum and minimum durations, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a woman who sees no postpartum bleeding, applying the ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Nifas is blood after childbirth, max 40 days, no min; if no blood, the correct view is she is not nifas' but ghusl is precautionary.

In this lesson, we will examine the legal definition of nifas (postpartum bleeding) according to the Hanafi school. We will clarify the precise moment nifas begins—based on the exit of most of the child—and its maximum duration of forty days, with no minimum. We will also address an important intra-Hanafi difference regarding a woman who gives birth but experiences no bleeding: is she considered in nifas or not?

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Nifas is blood that exits from the vagina after childbirth or after most of the child emerges, even if a miscarriage with formed limbs. If the child comes head-first, nifas starts after the head; if breech, after the navel.
The blood must come from the vagina; if from a cesarean wound, it is not nifas.
2
Determine the start of nifas
Nifas begins when the blood exits. For a normal delivery, blood after the head is nifas; for breech, after the navel. The woman becomes nufasā' only when blood is present.
Muhammad and Zafar required the entire child to exit, but the relied-upon view is that most is sufficient.
3
State the duration
Maximum is forty days, based on the hadith that the Prophet gave the nufasā' forty days unless she sees purity earlier. There is no minimum; any bleeding after birth is nifas.
If bleeding stops before 40 days, she performs ghusl and is pure. If it recurs within 40 days, it is still nifas.
4
Analyze the dispute on no blood
If a woman delivers but sees no blood, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold she is not nufasā' and only needs wudu. Imam Abu Hanifa said she must perform ghusl as a precaution. The majority of later scholars (mashāyikh) follow Imam Abu Hanifa, making precautionary ghusl the mufta bihi.
The dispute is one of caution (ihtiyat) vs. qiyas. Both positions are valid, but the operative fatwa follows Abu Hanifa.
