And it is prohibited for them (to perform) tawaf of the Kaaba, even if it is valid, because purification in it is a condition of perfection, and it (tawaf) releases one from ihram. And upon her (the menstruating woman) is a badanah (camel) in the tawaf of the rukn, and upon one in minor ritual impurity is a sheep, unless it is repeated with purification, due to the honor of the House and because tawaf around it is like prayer as reported in the Sunnah. And it is prohibited during menstruation and postpartum bleeding: intercourse and enjoyment of what is below the navel to below the knee, due to His statement, the Exalted, 'Do not approach them until they become pure,' and his statement (ﷺ): 'For you is what is above the lower garment.' If he had intercourse with her... This is the general ruling. The Messenger of Allah and ‘Ali: so it is permissible for them to stay in a state of janabah due to his statement: 'O ‘Ali, it is not lawful for anyone in a state of janabah to stay in this mosque except me and you.' Narrated by al-Tirmidhi, who said it is hasan gharib, and it has multiple chains. His statement: 'And it prohibits them from tawaf, even supererogatory.' His statement: 'Because purification' i.e., from the two types of hadath is a condition of perfection, meaning that validity does not depend on it, so it does not contradict its obligation, and thus permissibility is not lost by its loss, as in al-Burhan and others. Al-Kamal said: The essential consideration in prohibiting tawaf is the obligation of purification in it, not that it is in the mosque, so even if there were no mosque, tawaf would still be prohibited for her. His statement: 'And upon the one with hadath' i.e., in the tawaf of the rukn, otherwise it is charity. His statement: 'Unless it is repeated with purification' i.e., nothing is due if the repetition occurs during the days of sacrifice; otherwise, a dam is required due to delaying it. His statement: 'Due to the honor of the House' i.e., not because it is in the mosque, and it is the cause for his statement 'And it prohibits them from tawaf.' Al-‘Allamah Miskin said: He mentioned tawaf even though the prohibition of entering the mosque suffices, to refute the misconception that since standing at ‘Arafah is permissible without purification, even though it is the strongest pillar of Hajj, then tawaf would be more deserving of permissibility, or the misconception of entering the mosque for the necessity of tawaf. And you have known what al-Kamal said. 6. His statement: 'And enjoyment of what is below the navel.' As for the navel and above, enjoyment of it is permissible through intercourse or otherwise, even without a barrier, and similarly what is between the navel and knee with a barrier and without intercourse, even if stained with blood. What is prohibited is direct contact and touching, even without desire, not looking, even with desire, because it is not greater than kissing her face with desire, as stated in al-Bahr, and the author of al-Nahr discussed it in a way that is not complete. And just as the act is prohibited for him, it is prohibited for her to allow it. And it is permissible for him to kiss her and lie with her, and it is not disliked for her to cook or use what she has touched of dough, water, or other things, unless she performed wudu with the intention of drawing close (to Allah). And it is not appropriate to practice coitus interruptus with his wife because it resembles the action of the Jews, as in al-Bahr. And what is mentioned in al-Musannaf is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and upon it is the fatwa. And Muhammad specified the prohibition to the place of blood, which is the site of its exit, as in al-Jawharah fi Sharh al-Ta'wilat, and we adopt the view of Muhammad, and the author of al-Ghayah preferred it, and you have known what the fatwa is upon. And it is not permissible for a woman to conceal menstruation from her husband so that he may have intercourse with her without his knowledge, and it is also not permissible for her to pretend to be menstruating when she is not in order to prevent him from having intercourse with her, because it is prohibited. And if she informs him of menstruation, some said: If she is sinful, her statement is not accepted, but if she is chaste, it is accepted. And some said: If it is possible that she is truthful, such as if it is during the usual time of menstruation, it is accepted, even if she is sinful. The dispute: If he is not a permissible person, it is recommended that he give charity of a dinar or half of it and repent and not return. And it was definitively stated in al-Mabsut and others that the one who considers it permissible becomes an unbeliever, and it was corrected in al-Khulasah that he is not an unbeliever because it is prohibited due to something else. And the prohibition of intercourse with a woman in postpartum bleeding is explicitly stated, and I have not seen the ruling on whether it renders him an unbeliever or not.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi deals with the rulings of major ritual impurity (janabah), menstruation (hayd), and postpartum bleeding (nifas). The author begins by stating the general prohibitions for these states: staying in the mosque, performing tawaf, and engaging in intimate relations. The ruling for tawaf is that it is prohibited though valid if done, but requires a penalty (damm or sadaqah). The commentary explains that the prohibition of tawaf is due to the obligation of purification in it, not merely because it is in the mosque. Then the focus shifts to the specific prohibitions during hayd and nifas: intercourse and enjoyment of the area between the navel and knee. The Quranic verse 'Do not approach them until they become pure' is cited as the primary proof. The hadith 'For you is what is above the lower garment' indicates that the husband may enjoy the upper part of the body. The commentary elaborates on what is permitted and prohibited: the navel and above are permissible without barrier; the area between navel and knee is permissible only with a barrier and without intercourse. Direct contact and touching are prohibited, even without desire, but looking is not considered as serious. The wife must not allow the prohibited acts. The author then discusses the ruling of coitus interruptus (which is disliked and resembles the act of Jews) and the importance of the wife not lying about her menstruation to prevent or allow intercourse. Finally, there is a discussion of the expiation for intercourse during menstruation: a dinar or half dinar, with different opinions on the amount based on the color of blood, and a dispute over whether the one who considers it permissible becomes an unbeliever. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that it is not unbelief because the prohibition is due to an external factor (the blood), not inherent impurity.

Key Terms
الاستمتاع (al-istimtā‘): Enjoyment or sexual contact between spouses, including touching, kissing, and intercourse.
الوطء (al-waṭ’): Sexual intercourse, specifically penetration.
الحيض (al-ḥayḍ): Menstrual bleeding that occurs in healthy women for at least 3 days and at most 10 days.
النفاس (al-nifās): Postpartum bleeding after childbirth, which is like hayd in its rulings except for the duration (maximum 40 days).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a husband touching his wife's thighs during her menstruation if a barrier (like a cloth) is used?
A) It is prohibited (haram) under all circumstances.
B) It is permissible (mubah) according to the view of Imam Muhammad, which is preferred for fatwa.
C) It is disliked (makruh) but valid.
D) It is obligatory (wajib) to avoid all contact.

Key Takeaway
During hayd and nifas, intercourse and direct contact with the bloodied area are haram, but affectionate contact above the navel or with a barrier is permissible according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Intercourse During Menses: Charity and the Takfir Dispute
Ruling on intercourse during menses: recommended charity and dispute on takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between haram li-dhatihi and haram li-ghayrihi to evaluate the takfir of one who deems intercourse during menses permissible.

Core Idea
A mustahill of a haram li-ghayrihi act is not necessarily a kafir; the act's prohibition is contingent on the external impurity.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses the legal consequences for a man who has intercourse with his menstruating wife. While the act itself is haram, the text focuses on two distinct issues: the recommended expiation (charity) for one who does not deem it permissible, and the controversy over whether one who does deem it permissible becomes a disbeliever. The key lies in the Hanafi classification of the prohibition as haram li-ghayrihi, which influences the ruling on takfir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between ghayr mustahill (does not deem it permissible) and mustahill (deems it permissible). Note the ruling for each.
For ghayr mustahill: mustahabb charity and repentance. For mustahill: ikhtilaf on takfir.
2
Analyze the evidence for charity
Examine the hadith from Abu Dawud and al-Hakim regarding the charity amount. Apply Hanafi usul to determine that the imperative indicates recommendation (mandub), not obligation.
The hadith is ahad, so the ruling of haram remains unchanged; the charity is a sunnan.
3
Examine the takfir dispute
Compare the positions of al-Mabsut (takfir of mustahill) and al-Khulasa (no takfir). Identify the underlying principle: haram li-dhatihi vs. haram li-ghayrihi.
Al-Khulasa argues that since the prohibition is due to menstrual impurity (external factor), denying it does not amount to kufr.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Assess which position is relied upon. Al-Tahtawi's hashya presents al-Khulasa's view as the preferred, more cautious position.
The later Hanafi school avoids takfir in such cases, reflecting the maxim 'al-istihsan yashhad bi-annahu haram li-ghayrihi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مستحل له يستحب أن يتصدق بدينار أو نصفه ويتوب ولا يعود وجزم في المبسوط وغيره بكفر مستحله وصحح في الخلاصة عدم كفره لأنه حرام لغيره وحرمة وطء النفساء مصرح به ولم أر الحكم في تكفيره وعدمه، وهذا أحوط، وأقرب إلى الورع قوله : (يستحب أن يتصدق بدينار أو نصفه) قيل إن كان الدم اسود تصدق بدينار، وإن كان أصفر فينصفه، ويشهد له ما رواه أبو داود وصححه الحاكم إذا واقع الرجل أهله وهي حائض إن كان دماً أحمر فليتصدق بدينار وإن كان أصفر فبنصف دينار، وقيل إن كان في أول الحيض فبدينار وإلا فينصفه قوله : وصحح في الخلاصة عدم كفرة) تقدم ما فيه
For one who does not consider it permissible, it is recommended to give charity of a dinar or half, and to repent and not return. In al-Mabsut and others, it is asserted that the one who considers it permissible is a disbeliever. Al-Khulasa corrected that he is not a disbeliever because it is forbidden due to something else. The prohibition of intercourse with a woman in post-natal bleeding is explicitly stated, and I have not seen the ruling about his disbelief or not. This is more cautious and closer to piety. His statement: 'It is recommended to give charity of a dinar or half.' It is said that if the blood is black, he gives a dinar; if yellow, half a dinar. This is supported by what Abu Dawud narrated and al-Hakim authenticated: 'When a man has intercourse with his wife while she is menstruating, if the blood is red, let him give a dinar; if yellow, half a dinar.' And it is said that if it is at the beginning of menstruation, then a dinar; otherwise half. His statement: 'And al-Khulasa corrected that he is not a disbeliever' preceded what is in it.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-evidence on the consequences for a man who has intercourse with his menstruating wife. The core ruling is that for a man who does not regard the act as permissible (ghayr mustahill), it is mustahabb (recommended) to give a dinar or half in charity, to repent, and to resolve not to repeat the act. The text then notes a difference of opinion regarding a man who does consider it permissible (mustahill): according to al-Mabsut (a foundational work in the Hanafi school), such a person becomes a disbeliever (kafir); however, the later work al-Khulasa corrects that he is not a kafir, reasoning that the prohibition is haram li-ghayrihi (due to an external factor, like impurity) rather than haram li-dhatihi (intrinsically evil). The commentary explains the charity hadith in detail, linking the amount to the colour of the blood (black/red = a dinar; yellow = half) and the stage of menstruation. The author, al-Tahtawi, presents this dispute without fully resolving it, but the reliance on al-Khulasa suggests the non-takfir view is preferred in later practice.

Key Terms
مستحل (mustahill): One who considers an act prohibited by Islamic law to be permissible.
حرام لغيره (haram li-ghayrihi): Forbidden due to an external factor, not because the act itself is inherently evil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must give in charity a dinar or half, repent, and not return.
B) He becomes a kafir automatically.
C) No expiation is required.
D) He must perform ghusl only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recommends charity for intercourse during menses but does not automatically excommunicate one who deems it permissible; the prohibition's nature (haram li-ghayrihi) prevents takfir.

When Intercourse Becomes Permissible After Menses and Nifas: The Hanafi Position with Two Scenarios
Rulings on when intercourse becomes halal after cessation of menses/nifas with two scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scenarios of cessation of menses/nifas and state the conditions required for intercourse to become permissible in each scenario, citing the Hanafi proof from the Quran.

Core Idea
Intercourse is permissible without ghusl after maximum cessation; for less, ghusl, tayammum, or prayer-debt is required.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses a key question: when can a husband resume marital intimacy after his wife's menses or post-natal bleeding ends? The Hanafi school outlines a nuanced ruling that depends on whether the bleeding stopped at its maximum duration (10 days for hayd, 40 for nifas) or before that. The ruling is derived from the Quranic verse prohibiting approach until purification, which the Hanafis interpret through the two variant readings (qira'atayn). This lesson will guide you through the two scenarios and the legal reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between cessation at the maximum term (akthar) and cessation before it (dun al-akthar). Identify each scenario and its default ruling: permissible without ghusl for maximum, prohibited for less until conditions.
Maximum for hayd is 10 days, for nifas 40 days.
2
Examine the conditions for the second scenario
When cessation is less than maximum, three conditions lift the prohibition: ghusl, tayammum (with valid excuse), or passage of prayer time without purification (making prayer a debt). Note the detail: if cessation is less than her usual custom, she must wait until the custom passes even if she performs ghusl.
Ghusl before custom passes has no effect because return of blood is likely.
3
Analyze the proof from the Quran
The verse 'Do not approach them until they are purified' (2:222) is cited. The Hanafi interpretation uses the takhfif reading (without emphasis) to argue that 'purified' means cessation of blood at the maximum term, allowing intercourse without ghusl.
The reading with tashdid emphasizes purification through ghusl.
4
Reconcile the two Quranic readings
The companion reading with tashdid suggests that purification includes ghusl. The Hanafis reconcile by applying the tashdid to cessation before ten days (making ghusl required in that case) and the takhfif to cessation at ten days (allowing without ghusl). This shows the principle of giving effect to both proofs (al-jam' bayn al-dalilayn).
For the maximum case, ghusl is recommended, not obligatory.
5
Note nuances for Kitabi women and the concept of emphasis (mu'akkid)
For a non-Muslim wife, intercourse is permissible immediately upon cessation at her usual custom (within ten days) because she is not addressed by ghusl. The emphasis for tayammum via prayer is explained: tayammum needs a prayer to be validated, unlike ghusl.
This shows the operation of legal capacity (ahliyyah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And when the blood stops at the maximum of menses or post-natal bleeding, intercourse is permissible without ghusl due to His statement: 'Do not approach them until they are purified' with lightening of the ta, for He made purification the limit of prohibition. And it is recommended not to approach her until she performs ghusl due to the reading with tashdid, as a precaution. And post-natal bleeding is like menses. And it is not permissible for intercourse if the blood stops from a Muslim woman at less than the maximum, even if it is the completion of her usual period, except by one of three things: either she performs ghusl (because the time of ghusl in the minimum period is counted from the menses, and by ghusl she is freed from it; and if it stops less than her usual period, he should not approach her until her usual period passes, because its return is likely, so her ghusl before completion of her usual period has no effect), or she performs tayammum due to an excuse... his statement: 'excuse' meaning from the excuses that permit tayammum. And she should pray on the soundest view to emphasize tayammum for prayer, even if supererogatory, unlike ghusl which does not need emphasis. The third is mentioned in his statement: 'or the prayer becomes a debt in her obligation, and that is when after the cessation for completion of her usual period, she finds from the time when the blood stopped a time sufficient for ghusl and takbir and more, but she did not perform ghusl nor tayammum until the time exited; then by its mere exit her intercourse becomes permissible because the prayer of that time becomes due in her obligation, and this is a ruling from the rulings of purification. If the time is small, not sufficient for ghusl and takbir, her purification is not judged by its exit apart from purification with water or tayammum, so that the evening prayer does not become obligatory, and the fast of that day is not valid, as if she had become in the morning with menses. We restricted to the Muslim woman because the Kitabi woman's intercourse becomes permissible by the mere cessation of her blood for completion of her usual period before ten days due to her not being addressed with ghusl. And we only required the emphasis for cessation less than the maximum as a reconciliation between the two readings: for the reading with lightening permits intercourse after cessation before ghusl, and the reading with tashdid prohibits it before ghusl. So we applied the tashdid to less than ten days and the lightening to ten days. However, since the reading with tashdid apparently bears an absolute meaning, we said ghusl is recommended. And one who says there is no permissibility at all due to the tashdid reading would be abandoning one of the two proofs, while we act by both because the principle with proofs is to give effect, not to disregard.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling-with-proof regarding when intercourse becomes permissible after the cessation of menstrual (hayd) or post-natal (nifas) bleeding. It presents two key scenarios. First, if the bleeding stops at the maximum term (10 days for hayd, 40 for nifas), intercourse is halal without ghusl, based on the Quranic verse 'Do not approach them until they are purified' read with lightening (takhfif). However, it remains mustahabb (recommended) to wait until ghusl due to the alternative reading with tashdid. Second, if the bleeding stops before the maximum (even if it completes the woman's usual period), intercourse is not permissible until one of three conditions is met: (1) The woman performs ghusl; this is because the time of ghusl is counted as part of the menses period in the case of early cessation, and ghusl signals the end of impurity. If the blood stops before her usual custom, one must wait until that custom passes, as the return of blood is likely. (2) She performs tayammum due to a valid excuse; tayammum is emphasized by performing a prayer (even supererogatory) to validate it, unlike ghusl which requires no such emphasis. (3) The prayer time passes without her having performed ghusl or tayammum, so that the prayer becomes a debt in her obligation; upon the time's exit, intercourse becomes permissible because the obligation of prayer serves as a marker of purification. The passage further explains that for a Kitabi (Jewish/Christian) woman, intercourse is permissible upon the mere cessation of blood at the full custom, as she is not addressed by the obligation of ghusl. The Hanafi school reconciles the two Quranic readings by applying the tashdid to cessation before ten days (making ghusl mandatory in that case but interpreted as recommendation for the full term) and the takhfif to cessation at ten days (allowing intercourse without ghusl). Thus, both proofs are given effect.

Key Terms
أكثر الحيض (akthar al-hayd): The maximum duration of menses, which is ten days in the Hanafi school.
القراءتين (al-qira'atayn): The two variant Quranic readings: one with lightening (takhfif) and one with emphasis (tashdid) on the word 'yathurna'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is intercourse permissible without ghusl after menses according to the Hanafi school?
A) When blood stops at the maximum duration (10 days for menses).
B) Only after performing ghusl or tayammum.
C) After the woman has prayed one obligatory prayer.
D) Never; ghusl is always required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits intercourse without ghusl when menses/nifas stops at its maximum duration, based on the takhfif Quranic reading; for earlier cessation, ghusl, tayammum, or prayer-debt is required, with the added condition of waiting until the custom passes if cessation is before it.

Qada of Fasts Not Prayers for Menstruating and Postpartum Women
Ruling with proof from hadith and ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for menstruating women and cite the primary evidence (hadith and ijma) that supports it.

Core Idea
Menstruating women repay fasts, not prayers — based on explicit command and consensus.

This lesson examines a well-known fiqh ruling that distinguishes between prayers and fasts for women experiencing menses or postpartum bleeding. The ruling is derived from a clear prophetic practice confirmed by scholarly consensus. Understanding the evidence will help you appreciate how the Hanafi school uses ahad hadith and ijma to establish a settled rule.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify exactly what the menstruating or postpartum woman must and must not make up. The text clearly states: she repays the fast but not the prayer.
The ruling applies to both hayd (menses) and nifas (postpartum bleeding).
2
Identify the primary evidence
The hadith of Aisha (Radhiya Allahu 'anha) and the consensus (ijma) are cited. Recognize that the hadith is the main dalil, and ijma confirms it.
The hadith is reported in Sahih Muslim and other collections.
3
Analyze the proof's indication
The explicit command 'we were commanded to repay the fast' indicates obligation (wujub) for fasts. The absence of command for prayers indicates that they are not required to be repaid.
In Hanafi usul, a command (amr) indicates obligation unless there is a contrary indicator.
4
Confirm the authority
The text states 'and there is consensus upon it,' indicating that this ruling is agreed upon by the scholars. Within the Hanafi school there is no ikhtilaf; it is the mufta bihi position.
Consensus (ijma) is a binding source in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقضي الحائض والنفساء الصوم دون الصلاة لحديث رضي الله عنها كان يصيبنا ذلك فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة وعليه الإجماع
And the menstruating and postpartum woman repays the fast but not the prayer, due to the hadith of 'Radhiya Allahu 'anha: 'That used to befall us, so we were commanded to repay the fast and not commanded to repay the prayer,' and there is consensus upon it.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This is a concise ruling-with-proof regarding the obligations of a woman after menses (hayd) or post-natal bleeding (nifas). The core ruling is that she must make up (qada') the fasts she missed during that time, but not the prayers. The evidence is a hadith from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) in which she says that when they experienced menstruation during the time of the Prophet (ﷺ), they were commanded to make up the fasts but not the prayers. The text adds that this ruling is supported by consensus (ijma'). This is a well-established position in the Hanafi school and across the majority of scholars. The wisdom is that requiring repayment of all missed prayers would cause undue hardship, as menses recurs monthly and the number of missed prayers would accumulate greatly.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up an obligatory act missed due to a valid excuse after the excuse has ceased.
إجماع (ijma'): The consensus of Muslim scholars on a ruling, considered a primary source of Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a woman make up after her menstruation ends according to the Hanafi school?
A) Both prayers and fasts.
B) Only prayers.
C) Only fasts.
D) Neither prayers nor fasts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that only fasts are made up after menses is firmly established by hadith and consensus.

Tayammum is Emphasized for Prayer
Explains the emphasis on tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that tayammum is emphasized (wajib) for prayer to confirm the prayer's liability, and distinguish this from ghusl which needs no emphasis.

Core Idea
Tayammum is stressed for prayer; ghusl needs no stress because its obligation is clear.

In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle point: while ghusl is required for major impurity without needing any additional emphasis, tayammum—being a substitute—requires an emphasis (ta'kid) to ensure that the prayer becomes a confirmed obligation on the person. This distinction is crucial for understanding Hanafi rulings on purification and prayer liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is tayammum for prayer. State its hukm level: according to the correct view (al-ashah), tayammum is emphasized (wajib) for prayer, even for supererogatory prayers.
This emphasis is called 'ta'kid' in Hanafi terminology.
2
Compare with ghusl
Note that ghusl does not require such emphasis. Why? Because ghusl is the primary purification for major hadath, and its obligation is already established directly from the Quran and Sunnah.
Tayammum is a substitute, so its ruling needs reinforcement to be comparable.
3
Understand the consequence
This emphasis ensures that the prayer becomes a debt (dayn) in her liability if she delays purification until time exits. This sets the stage for the next ruling on intercourse.

Source Text

He said: ('From the time in which the blood stopped, etc.') meaning that it is from the five times. So if it stopped at the time of Duha, and she did not perform ghusl after it and did not perform tayammum, her intercourse is not permissible until the time of Zuhr exits, so that her prayer is established in her liability by its exit, because the time before noon is a neglected time with no consideration for its exit. He said: ('Sufficient for ghusl and the takbir') al-Mujtaba said: The correct is that along with ghusl, wearing clothes is considered, and similarly the answer for her fast if she becomes pure before dawn, but the correct is that the takbir is not considered in regard to fasting, and the time of takbir from Zuhr is in any case. He said: ('and what is above them, its ruling is known a fortiori from before') He said: ('and it is a ruling from the rulings of purifications') i.e., so all other rulings follow it, including the permissibility of intercourse. Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) from the Quran (the two readings) and demonstrates the Hanafi position on tayammum and the permissibility of intercourse after menstruation. The author explains that for a menstruating woman to perform tayammum for prayer is emphasized (on the correct view) because it serves as a reminder that a prayer has become due. This is unlike ghusl, which does not require such emphasis. The ruling then discusses when she becomes permissible for intercourse: if her period ends and she has enough time to perform ghusl and the takbirat al-ihram but does not do so until the time exits, then by the mere exit of that prayer time, her intercourse becomes permissible because the prayer has now become a debt (dhan) in her liability. However, if the time was insufficient for ghusl and takbir, then the exit of the time does not make her pure, and she is not liable for that prayer. The text restricts this to Muslim women because a Kitabiyya (Jewish/Christian woman) becomes permissible simply by the cessation of blood before ten days, as she is not addressed with ghusl. The author also reconciles two Quranic readings: one that permits intercourse after cessation before ghusl and another that forbids it. The Hanafi school reconciles by applying the stricter reading to cases where the total menstruation is less than ten days (indicating the need for ghusl) and the lighter reading to cases of exactly ten days, but still recommends ghusl. The commentary elaborates on details such as the start time (the five prayer times), the need to wear clothes after ghusl, and the consideration of takbir for fasting vs. prayer.

Key Terms
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution using earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
مؤكد (mu'akkad): Emphasized, here referring to tayammum being stressed to mark the liability of prayer.

Tayammum is legally emphasized for prayer to firmly establish the prayer's obligation, unlike ghusl.

Intercourse After Menstruation: Time Exit and Quranic Readings
Explains conditions for intercourse after menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a menstruating woman becomes halal for intercourse when the prayer time exits after she had sufficient time for ghusl and takbir, and explain the reconciliation between the two Quranic readings.

Core Idea
Her prayer becomes a debt; the exit of time makes intercourse halal.

This passage delves into the detailed scenario: when a woman's period ends, she has an opportunity to purify for the upcoming prayer. If she neglects purification until the prayer time expires, the prayer becomes a debt (dayn) in her liability, and by that very exit, intercourse becomes permissible. The text also addresses the Quranic readings on this issue, reconciling them in a way that balances the two proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for permissibility
The passage states that if after her period ends, she has enough time to perform ghusl and the takbirat al-ihram, but she does not do so, then by the mere exit of the prayer time, she becomes permissible for intercourse.
The time must be sufficient for both ghusl and the takbir.
2
Understand the role of the prayer time
The exit of the prayer time renders the prayer a debt (dayn) in her liability. This debt is a ruling from the rulings of purification, which in turn permits intercourse.
3
Distinguish when this does not apply
If the time is insufficient for ghusl and takbir, then the exit of the time does not make her pure, and she is not liable for that prayer. For example, if blood stops shortly before sunrise with less than enough time for ghusl, she must wait until zuhr time exits.
4
Note the exception for Kitabiyya
A Jewish or Christian woman becomes permissible simply upon the cessation of blood before ten days, because she is not addressed with ghusl.
This is because the obligation of ghusl is not upon them.
5
Reconcile the two Quranic readings
The reading with tashdid (emphasis) suggests that ghusl is required before intercourse, while the reading with takhfif (light) suggests permissibility upon cessation. The Hanafis reconcile by applying the stricter reading to cases where the total menstruation is less than ten days, and the lighter reading to exactly ten days, but still recommend ghusl.
The principle is to act on both proofs as much as possible.

We restricted to the Muslim woman because the Kitabiyya's intercourse becomes permissible by the mere cessation of her blood for her complete habit before ten days due to lack of address to her regarding ghusl. And we only stipulated the emphasis for cessation for less than the maximum, reconciling between the two readings. He said: ('And she prays' upon the correct view) for mere tayammum does not stand in place of ghusl in this chapter by consensus upon the correct view, as in al-Bahr, due to what the author mentioned. He said: ('From the time in which the blood stopped, etc.') meaning that it is from the five times. So if it stopped at the time of Duha, and she did not perform ghusl after it and did not perform tayammum, her intercourse is not permissible until the time of Zuhr exits, so that her prayer is established in her liability by its exit, because the time before noon is a neglected time with no consideration for its exit. Similarly, if it stopped shortly before sunrise with less than her ability to perform ghusl and the takbir, her intercourse is not permissible until the time of Zuhr exits, as stated in al-Sharh. He said: ('Sufficient for ghusl and the takbir') al-Mujtaba said: The correct is that along with ghusl, wearing clothes is considered, and similarly the answer for her fast if she becomes pure before dawn, but the correct is that the takbir is not considered in regard to fasting, and the time of takbir from Zuhr is in any case. He said: ('and what is above them, its ruling is known a fortiori from before') He said: ('and it is a ruling from the rulings of purifications') i.e., so all other rulings follow it, including the permissibility of intercourse. He said: ('or tayammum') i.e., with its condition. He said: ('due to lack of address to her regarding ghusl') This is one of corrected sayings, among them the saying that she is addressed in performance and belief, so her ruling is like that of the Muslim woman. He said: ('reconciling between the two readings') for the reading with tashdid forbids intercourse after cessation before ghusl, and the reading with takhfif permits it before ghusl, so we interpreted the tashdid as for less than ten days and the takhfif for ten days, except that since the apparent of the tashdid reading may imply absoluteness, we said that ghusl is recommended, and whoever says that it is not permissible at all due to the tashdid reading abandons acting on one of the two proofs, and we act on both because the principle in proofs is acting upon them, not neglecting them.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) from the Quran (the two readings) and demonstrates the Hanafi position on tayammum and the permissibility of intercourse after menstruation. The author explains that for a menstruating woman to perform tayammum for prayer is emphasized (on the correct view) because it serves as a reminder that a prayer has become due. This is unlike ghusl, which does not require such emphasis. The ruling then discusses when she becomes permissible for intercourse: if her period ends and she has enough time to perform ghusl and the takbirat al-ihram but does not do so until the time exits, then by the mere exit of that prayer time, her intercourse becomes permissible because the prayer has now become a debt (dhan) in her liability. However, if the time was insufficient for ghusl and takbir, then the exit of the time does not make her pure, and she is not liable for that prayer. The text restricts this to Muslim women because a Kitabiyya (Jewish/Christian woman) becomes permissible simply by the cessation of blood before ten days, as she is not addressed with ghusl. The author also reconciles two Quranic readings: one that permits intercourse after cessation before ghusl and another that forbids it. The Hanafi school reconciles by applying the stricter reading to cases where the total menstruation is less than ten days (indicating the need for ghusl) and the lighter reading to cases of exactly ten days, but still recommends ghusl. The commentary elaborates on details such as the start time (the five prayer times), the need to wear clothes after ghusl, and the consideration of takbir for fasting vs. prayer.

Key Terms
القراءتين (al-qira'atayn): The two Quranic readings: one with tashdid (emphasis on 't' in yatahharna) and one with takhfif (light pronunciation 'yat'hirna').
دينا في ذمتها (daynan fi dhimmatiha): A debt in her liability, meaning that the prayer becomes an obligatory duty that must be fulfilled later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a Muslim woman become permissible for intercourse according to the Hanafi school if she has sufficient time for ghusl and takbir but delays?
A) Immediately upon cessation of blood.
B) Only after performing ghusl with water.
C) After the prayer time exits, when the prayer becomes a debt in her liability.
D) After she performs tayammum.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits intercourse after menstruation without ghusl only when the prayer time exits with a prayer debt, reconciling two Quranic readings.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Prohibition of Touching the Quran for a Person in Hadath
states the ruling and proof for prohibition, with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching the Quran while in hadath, cite the Quranic proof, explain the exception with a cover, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Touching the Quran is haram for a muhdith; a cover permits it.

This lesson focuses on a core ruling in taharah: the prohibition of touching the Quran for anyone without wudu. We will examine the primary evidence from the Quran and the Hanafi interpretation, the exception for covers, and the cross-madhhab difference with the Shafi'i school. Understanding this ruling is essential for daily practice and highlights the Hanafi method of deriving hukum from nass.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is prohibited? The act of a muhdith (person in hadath) touching the Quran. The ruling is haram, with an exception for when a cover (ghilaf) is used.
The text also mentions prohibition of prayer and tawaf for muhdith, focusing on touching Quran.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the Quranic verse, Surah al-Waqi'ah: 79, 'None touch it except the purified.' The text also alludes to a hadith (not quoted).
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala – the Hanafi school interprets 'mutahharun' as those free from hadath.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The verse is addressed to the Quran itself, saying only the purified may touch it. The Hanafi school takes 'purified' to mean those in a state of wudu or ghusl, hence touching without purification is prohibited.
Other schools may interpret 'purified' as meaning angels or specifically those free from major impurity; the Hanafi view includes both minor and major hadath.
4
Examine the exception
The text explicitly permits touching with a cover (ghilaf). This is because the cover breaks direct contact with the mushaf, and the ruling is based on the act of touching the Quran itself, not its cover.
The cover can be a cloth, box, or anything that separates the hand from the text.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that wudu is not required for touching the Quran; rather, only being free from major hadath (janabah) is required. For minor hadath, touching is permitted. The Hanafi school disagrees, requiring wudu for any hadath.
This difference stems from varying interpretations of 'mutahharun' and whether the verse implies a command or a statement of fact.

Source Text

فروع يكره كتابة قرآن أو إسم الله تعالى على ما يفرش لما فيه من ترك التعظيم، وكذا على درهم، ومحراب وجدار لما يخاف من سقوط الكتابة. تابوت وضع فيه كتب فالأدب أن لا يضع عليه الثياب، وفي الخلاصة مد الرجلين إلى جانب المصحف إذا لم يكن بحذائه لا يكره، وكذا لو كان المصحف معلقاً بالوتد وهو ماذا الرجلين إلى جانب المصحف لا يكره، ولا بأس بوضع مقلمة على كتاب أو مصحف لأجل الكتابة وإلا كره. وضع شيئاً مكتوباً فيه إسم الله تعالى تحت طنفسة كره الجلوس عليها، وقال صاحب الهداية لا يكره أما لو جعل المصحف في الجوالق وهو يركب عليه لا بأس به للحفظ، ولغير الحفظ يكره اهـ.
Chapter of Purification. The Quran) because of the prohibition of touching it except with a cover) because of the prohibition by text, and entering the mosque of Tawaf) because of the preceding text. And blood of istihadah) is blood from a burst vein, not from the womb, its sign is that it has no odor, and its ruling is like continuous nosebleed, it does not prevent prayer) meaning it does not remove the obligation nor prevent its validity if it continues flowing for a complete time as we will mention (and) it does not prevent (fasting) whether obligatory or voluntary (and) it does not prohibit (intercourse) because it is not harm. (And) the purification of those with excuses is necessary; she clarified it by saying: the mustahadah performs wudu) and she is one whose blood is less than the minimum of menstruation or exceeds the maximum, or exceeds the maximum of postpartum bleeding, or exceeds her habit in its minimum and exceeds its maximum, and the pregnant woman and one who has not reached nine years, and one who has an excuse like urinary incontinence, diarrhea, passing wind, and continuous nosebleed that does not stop and cannot be stopped by packing without hardship, nor by sitting, nor by making gestures in prayer; so with this they perform wudu for the time, not for every prayer nor for voluntary (prayers), because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The mustahadah performs wudu for the time of every prayer' narrated by Sabt Ibn al-Jawzi from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. So all those with excuses are in the ruling of the mustahadah, so the evidence includes them. And they pray) with it, i.e., with their wudu in that time, whatever they wish of obligatory prayers, performing current ones and making up missed ones even if they became due during a time of health, and whatever they wish of voluntary prayers. Branches: It is disliked to write Quran or the name of Allah on what is spread, because it involves lack of respect; likewise on a dirham, a mihrab, and a wall, due to fear of the writing falling. A chest in which books are placed, the proper conduct is not to place clothes on it. In al-Khulasa: stretching the legs towards the side of the Quran if it is not directly opposite is not disliked; likewise if the Quran is hung on a peg and one stretches the legs towards its side, it is not disliked. There is no harm in placing a pen case on a book or Quran for the purpose of writing, otherwise it is disliked. Placing something on which the name of Allah is written under a prayer mat is disliked to sit on it, and the author of al-Hidayah said it is not disliked. However, if the Quran is placed in a saddlebag and one rides on it, there is no harm for preservation; for other than preservation, it is disliked. End.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya is a critical refinement of the matn of Maraqi al-Falah, expanding on the rulings of purification. The passage begins by stating the prohibition for a person in hadath to touch the Quran, with the evidence being the Quranic verse (56:79) and the hadith. The exception is touching with a cover, such as a cloth or sheath. The text then defines istihadah (chronic non-menstrual bleeding) and rules that it does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations, unlike menstruation. The mustahadah and others with chronic excuses (such as urinary incontinence, diarrhea, constant wind, and continuous nosebleed) perform wudu for each prayer time and can then pray any obligatory or voluntary prayers during that time. The branches (furu') discuss etiquettes: it is disliked to write Quran on items that may be treated disrespectfully, and various rulings on placing the Quran and items containing Allah's name. The Hanafi position is that these rulings are based on the need for reverence.

Key Terms
محدث (muhdith): A person in a state of minor or major ritual impurity (hadath)
غلاف (ghilaf): A cover or sheath that separates the hand from the Quranic text

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Halal (permitted) because minor hadath does not require wudu for touching
B) Makruh (disliked) but not haram
C) Haram (prohibited) based on the Quranic verse
D) Permitted only if the person is not in janabah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds it haram to touch the Quran without wudu, based on the Quran and Sunnah, with a valid exception for covers.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Rulings for Mustahadah and Persons with Chronic Excuses in Taharah
defines istihadah and gives rulings, with branches on reverence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define istihadah, explain its impact on prayer, fasting, and marital relations, identify the rules for performing wudu for those with chronic excuses, and apply the branches on reverence for the Quran.

Core Idea
Istihadah does not prevent worship; those with chronic excuses perform wudu once per prayer time.

This lesson covers the fiqh of mustahadah and other individuals with chronic excuses (sahib al-udhr). The text defines istihadah, distinguishes it from menstruation, and establishes that it does not obstruct prayer, fasting, or marital relations. A key rule is that such individuals perform wudu for the time of each prayer and can then pray any number of prayers. The lesson also includes several branches on proper reverence for the Quran and the name of Allah, highlighting the comprehensive nature of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define istihadah and its signs
Identify what istihadah is: blood from a burst vein, not from the womb. Its sign is that it has no odor. Contrast with menstrual blood.
Istihadah is chronic, non-cyclical bleeding that does not follow the patterns of menstruation or postpartum.
2
Identify the rulings: not preventing worship
Istihadah does not prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations. This is a key difference from menstruation. The text explicitly states this.
Prayer and fasting remain obligatory; the mustahadah is treated like a person with a chronic excuse, not like a menstruating woman.
3
Explain the wudu rule for those with chronic excuses
The mustahadah and others like those with urinary incontinence perform wudu for the time of each obligatory prayer. This wudu allows them to pray any number of fard and nafl prayers during that time, as long as the excuse persists.
The wudu is for the time, not for each prayer. Once the time ends, the wudu breaks and must be renewed.
4
Apply the branches on reverence for the Quran
The branches (furu') cover rulings about writing Quran on various surfaces, placing items with Allah's name, stretching legs, and using Quran as a saddlebag. They emphasize maintaining respect and avoiding disrespect.
These branches are practical applications of the principle of reverence (ta'dhim) for the Quran and the name of Allah.

Taharah — Purification

Chronic Excuses and the Ruling on Wudu
Defines chronic excuses and states they make wudu once per prayer time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of chronic excuses and apply the ruling that one wudu per prayer time suffices for all prayers within that time.

Core Idea
One wudu per prayer time suffices for a person with a chronic excuse, based on the hadith of the mustahadah.

This lesson delves into a crucial concession in Islamic law for those suffering from continuous discharges that break wudu. The Hanafi school, relying on the prophetic model of the mustahadah, grants such individuals a single wudu for the entire time of a prayer, removing hardship while preserving the obligation to pray. Understanding this ruling requires knowing what qualifies as a chronic excuse and how the evidence from the Sunnah is applied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chronic excuses
Read the list of examples given: sals al-bawl (continuous dripping of urine), istiṭlāq al-baṭn (diarrhea), infilāt al-rīḥ (uncontrollable wind), ruʿāf dā'im (perpetual nosebleed), and similar conditions. Note the defining characteristic: the discharge is continuous and cannot be stopped without hardship.
The condition must be such that it would cause excessive difficulty to prevent.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is that the person makes wuḍū' once for the time of an obligatory prayer (e.g., the entire ẓuhr time). With that wuḍū', they may pray any number of obligatory and supererogatory prayers within that time.
This is a concession (rukhṣah) to alleviate hardship.
3
Examine the proof (dalīl)
The proof is the ḥadīth: 'Al-mustaḥāḍah tatawaḍḍa' li-waqti kulli ṣalāh.' The imperative indicates obligation (wujūb) according to Ḥanafī uṣūl, and the phrase 'li-waqti' shows the wuḍū' is for the time, not for each prayer.
The ḥadīth is from Sibṭ Ibn al-Jawzī and is considered aḥad, thus zannī al-thubūt, explaining why the ruling is wājib and not farḍ.
4
Derive the scope via qiyās
All chronic excuses are analogized to the mustaḥāḍah because the effective cause (ʿillah) is the same: continuous, uncontrollable discharge. Therefore, the same ruling applies to all such conditions.
This is a qiyās jaliyy (clear analogy).
5
Apply the conditions for being excused
The person must have reached an age where wuḍū' is valid (nine years for males), and the discharge must be such that it cannot be stopped without hardship. If it can be stopped by sitting or gesturing, the excuse is lifted.
Packing with cotton or similar is recommended or obligatory depending on necessity.

الجرح، وقيل كالحائض، لأن ما يخرج من السبيلين أشد من الخارج من غيرهما كذا في السراج وبحث بعضهم إلحاق السلس والإستطلاق بالإستحاضة للعلة المذكورة. قوله: (ولا بجلوس) أما إذا كان يمكنه رده بجلوس في الفرض ونحوه وجب رده به، وخرج عن أن يكون صاحب عذر اهـ من الشرح بزيادة قوله: (ولا بالإيماء في الصلاة) فإن امتنع به عذره تعين فعله لأن ترك السجود أهون من الصلاة الحدث قاله في الشرح قوله : لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم الخ) ولأنه لو بطل لفاتت الرخصة، ولزوم الحرج بخلاف طرق حدث آخر فإن الوضوء ينتقض به ولو في الوقت لعدم الضرورة. قوله : (تتوضأ لوقت كل صلاة) وهو محكم بالنسبة للحديث الآخر الوارد بلفظ لكل صلاة لأن الصلاة تطلق على الأفعال وعلى الوقت عرفاً
A person who reaches nine years of age and one who has an excuse such as continuous dripping of urine, or looseness of stomach, and escape of wind, and perpetual nosebleed that does not stop, cannot be stopped by packing without hardship, nor by sitting, nor by gesturing in prayer. So they perform wudu for the time of every (obligatory prayer), not for every prayer nor for supererogatory prayers. Due to the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'The mustahadah (woman with chronic uterine bleeding) performs wudu for the time of every prayer.' Reported by Sibṭ Ibn al-Jawzī from Abū Ḥanīfah. So all other people with chronic excuses are in the ruling of the mustahadah, so the evidence includes them. And they pray with it (i.e., with their wudu for that time) as many obligatory prayers as they wish—performance of time-bound ones and make-up for others, even if they were due during the time of health—and as many supererogatory prayers as they wish. His saying: 'like continuous dripping of urine' i.e., its flow; its owner is one whose dripping of urine does not cease due to weakness in his bladder or overpowering coldness. 'Sals' (with fatḥa on lām) is the excretion itself, and with kasra on lām is the person with this disease. His saying: 'and looseness of stomach' i.e., flowing of what is in it; it is a metonymy of locus for state. From al-Nahr. His saying: 'and escape of wind' i.e., sudden exit. His saying: 'and perpetual nosebleed' i.e., continuous, not stopping. It is with ḍamma on rā' the blood exiting from the nose. They say ra'afa yar'afu from the patterns naṣara and nafa'a. As for ra'ufa (like ḥasuna), it is a weak dialect according to al-Ṣiḥāḥ. 'Does not stop' i.e., does not settle. Similarly one who has ophthalmia, or trachoma, or pterygium and tears flow from him, and similarly everything that exits with pain even from ear, breast, or navel, because it is a nullifier of wudu due to coming out from a wound. His saying: 'and cannot be stopped etc.' So it is obligatory for him to stop it whenever he can by treatment without hardship. In al-Muḍmarāt from al-Niṣāb: someone with continuous dripping of urine: he places a cotton wick in his penis and prevents its exit, and he knows that if he did not pack, the urine would appear. If he removes the cotton and it has wetness, he is in a state of ḥadath at the moment of removing the cotton only, and this is the ruling for iftā'. If the excuse is not prevented by that, should he do it to reduce impurity as much as possible? They said: it is recommended. Ibn Amīr Ḥājj said: i.e., recommended because in al-Khulāṣah: if he does not do it, there is no harm. al-Ḥalabī said: i.e., obligatory. And they differed concerning the mustahādah if she packs: it is said she is like one with a wound, and it is said like a menstruating woman, because what comes out from the two passages is more severe than what comes out from elsewhere. This is in al-Sirāj. And some investigated analogy of sals and istiṭlāq to mustahādah for the mentioned cause. His saying: 'nor by sitting' - if it can be stopped by sitting in the obligatory and similar, it is obligatory to stop it by that, and he leaves the status of being a person with chronic excuse. From the sharḥ with addition. His saying: 'nor by gesturing in prayer' - if his excuse ceases by that, it is obligatory to do it, because abandoning prostration is easier than praying with ḥadath. He said this in the sharḥ. His saying: 'due to the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him etc.' and because if it were invalid, the concession would be lost, and hardship would be necessitated, contrary to the occurrence of another ḥadath, for wudu is nullified by it even within the time due to lack of necessity. His saying: 'She performs wudu for the time of every prayer' - this is authoritative over the other narration worded 'for every prayer' because 'ṣalāh' applies to actions and to time by custom.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Ḥanafī school regarding persons with chronic excuses (aṣḥāb al-aʿdhār). The text begins by listing common chronic excuses: continuous dripping of urine (sals al-bawl), diarrhea (istiṭlāq al-baṭn), uncontrollable wind (infilāt al-rīḥ), and perpetual nosebleed (ruʿāf dā'im). It adds that similar conditions like tears flowing from eye diseases or discharge from wounds also qualify. The key ruling is that such a person performs wuḍū' once for the entire time of an obligatory prayer (e.g., the time of ẓuhr), not for each prayer individually. With that single wuḍū', they may pray any number of obligatory prayers (including make-up prayers for those missed during health) and any number of supererogatory prayers. The proof is the ḥadīth of the mustaḥāḍah (woman with chronic uterine bleeding) who is commanded to perform wuḍū' for the time of each prayer, and analogically all other chronic excuses are treated the same. The commentary expands on each term: for sals, it distinguishes between the condition and the excretion; for ruʿāf, it clarifies the vowelization; it discusses the requirement to try to stop the discharge by means without hardship (e.g., packing with cotton for urinary incontinence). It mentions a difference of opinion on whether the mustaḥāḍah who packs is like one with a wound or like a menstruating woman, but the preferred view for sals is the packing ruling. It also notes that if sitting or nodding in prayer can stop the discharge, then one must do so and is no longer considered excused. The text also clarifies that the ḥadīth 'for the time of every prayer' is the authoritative text over another version 'for every prayer' because 'ṣalāh' can refer to the time of prayer by custom.

Key Terms
سلس (sals): In this text, sals (with fatḥa on lām) is the urine that drips continuously due to a medical condition; sals (with kasra on lām) is the person suffering from this condition.
استطلاق بطن (istiṭlāq baṭn): Literally 'looseness of the belly', i.e., diarrhea that is continuous and beyond control.
رعاف دائم (ruʿāf dā'im): Continuous nosebleed that does not stop and cannot be easily controlled.
مستحاضة (mustaḥāḍah): A woman with chronic uterine bleeding beyond her normal menstrual period; she is analogous to other chronic excuses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person with continuous dripping of urine (sals al-bawl) wants to pray Dhuhr and Asr within the Dhuhr time. According to the Hanafi school, how many wudus must he perform?
A) One wudu before Dhuhr and one before Asr
B) One wudu for the entire Dhuhr time, with which he can pray both
C) He cannot pray Asr with the same wudu because it is a different prayer
D) He must perform wudu for each rakah

Key Takeaway
A person with a chronic excuse performs one wudu for the entire time of an obligatory prayer, and with it may pray any number of prayers.

Conditions for Becoming a Ma'dhur (Chronic Excuse)
Details the conditions for ma'dhur status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi conditions for a person to be considered a ma'dhur and apply them to determine whether a person qualifies for the concession.

Core Idea
Three conditions: full time coverage without sufficient break, persistence, and no new nullifier — even of the same type.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi explains the precise legal criteria for a person with a chronic excuse (ma'dhur) in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding these conditions is crucial because the ma'dhur has special rules for wudu and prayer. The author unpacks the key terms and gives examples to clarify borderline cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial coverage condition
The excuse must cover an entire prayer time without a break sufficient for wudu and prayer. If such a break exists, the person is not ma'dhur.
This is the 'isti'āb al-waqt' condition.
2
Distinguish real vs. constructive coverage
Real coverage means the excuse is present throughout the entire time. Constructive coverage means there are short breaks that do not allow for wudu and prayer; the person is still considered covered.
Short breaks that cannot accommodate wudu and prayer are ignored.
3
Determine the condition for continuity
After the initial time, the excuse must exist in every subsequent prayer time, even if only once, to show its persistence.
One occurrence per time period suffices to establish continuity.
4
Analyze the nullifier rule
If a new nullifier occurs—even if it is similar to the chronic excuse—the wudu is invalidated. Each new instance is a separate ḥadath.
The examples of smallpox and nostril bleeding illustrate this.
5
Apply the rule to examples
A non-oozing wound that starts oozing is a new nullifier. Similarly, if one nostril is bleeding and the other starts, it breaks wudu.
This prevents the concession from being overextended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يصير من ابتلي بناقض معذوراً حتى يستوعبه العذر وقتاً كاملاً ليس فيه انقطاع لعذره بقدر الوضوء والصلاة) إذ لو وجد لا يكون معذوراً (وهذا الاستيعاب الحقيقي بوجود العذر في جميع الوقت والاستيعاب الحكمي بالانقطاع القليل الذي لا يسع الطهارة والصلاة (شرط ثبوته) أي العذر (وشرط دوامه) أي العذر (وجوده) أي العذر في كل وقت بعد ذلك الاستيعاب الحقيقي، أو الحكمي (ولو) كان وجوده (مرة واحدة ليعلم بها بقاؤه (وشرط انقطاعه) قوله : (إذا لم يطرأ ناقض غير العذر) فإن طرأ ولو كان نظير عذره نقضه حتى لو كان به دماً ميل أو جدري فتوضأ وبعضها سائل، ثم سال الذي لم يكن سائلاً إنتقض وضوءه لأن هذا حدث جديد فصار كما لو سال أحد منخريه فتوضأ مع سيلانه، وصلى ثم سال المنخر الآخر في الوقت انتقض وضوءه لأن هذا حدث جديد كما في الفتح. قوله : (عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد) متعلق بقوله : يبطل بعد تعلق قوله : بخروجه به . وهو
His saying: 'And one who is afflicted with a nullifier does not become a person with a chronic excuse until the excuse covers an entire time period in which there is no break in his excuse sufficient for wudu and prayer.' For if such a break exists, he is not a person with a chronic excuse. 'And this coverage is real (by existence of the excuse throughout the entire time) and constructive (by a short break that does not suffice for purification and prayer). (It is a condition for the establishment) of the excuse, and (a condition for its continuity) is its existence in every time after that real or constructive coverage, even if it exists once to know its persistence. And (a condition for its cessation) ...' His saying: 'if no nullifier other than the excuse occurs' - for if it occurs, even if it is similar to his excuse, it nullifies. So if someone has smallpox or chickenpox and performs wudu while some of them are oozing, then the one that was not oozing starts to ooze, his wudu is nullified because this is a new event. It becomes like if one of his nostrils was flowing, he performed wudu with its flow, then the other nostril started flowing within the same time, his wudu is nullified because it is a new event, as in al-Fatḥ. His saying: 'according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad' is attached to his saying 'it becomes null' after attaching his saying 'by its exit' to it.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions under which a person with a recurrent nullifier (like continuous urine) is considered a 'ma'dhur' (person with a chronic excuse) in Hanafi fiqh. The text states two key conditions: (1) the excuse must cover an entire time period (e.g., the time of Dhuhr) without a break long enough to perform wudu and the prayer; (2) for continuity, the excuse must exist at every subsequent time, even if only once to indicate its persistence. The commentary then addresses a scenario: if a person has smallpox (blood blisters) and some are oozing while others are not, when the non-oozing ones start oozing, that is a new event and nullifies the wudu, even though it is similar to the original excuse. This is because each occurrence is a separate hadath. The same applies if one nostril is bleeding and then the other starts bleeding during the prayer time. The passage also sets up the intra-Hanafi dispute about invalidation by time exit, which is detailed in the next lesson. The overall principle is that a ma'dhur's wudu is fragile: it remains valid only as long as no new nullifier (including a new instance of the same type of excuse) occurs.

Key Terms
استيعاب الوقت (isti'āb al-waqt): The condition that the chronic excuse covers the entire prayer time without a break sufficient for wudu and prayer; this can be real (continuous presence) or constructive (short breaks that don't suffice).
حدث جديد (ḥadath jadīd): A new occurrence of a nullifier, even if of the same type as the chronic excuse, which invalidates wudu.
انقطاع (inqiṭāʿ): A break in the excuse; if it lasts long enough to perform wudu and prayer, the person is not ma'dhur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man has continuous urine dripping for the entire Dhuhr time except for a 5-minute period during which he stops and can perform wudu and pray. According to Hanafi fiqh, is he considered a ma'dhur for that time?
A) Yes, because the excuse covers most of the time
B) No, because the break was sufficient for wudu and prayer
C) Only if the break was less than the time for one rakah
D) Yes, because he had the intention to be excused

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhur's wudu is valid only if the chronic excuse covers the full prayer time without sufficient break, persists in subsequent times, and no new nullifier occurs.

Invalidation of Wudu for a Ma'dhur: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Timing
States the ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on invalidation of wudu for a ma'dhur at time exit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on when the wudu of a ma'dhur becomes invalid, explain the conceptual basis of the relied-upon view, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
The wudu of a chronically excused person is time-bound; the key dispute is whether invalidation occurs at time exit, entry, or both.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the wudu of a person with a chronic excuse (ma'dhur). While the base text of Maraqi al-Falah states that the wudu becomes invalid at the exit of the prayer time, the commentary reveals three distinct positions among the Hanafi imams. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the concession (rukhsah) and for knowing when a new wudu is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what triggers the invalidation of the wudu of a ma'dhur: Is it the exit of the current prayer time, the entry of the next, or both?
The issue arises because the ma'dhur's wudu is valid despite continuous hadath due to a license.
2
List the three Hanafi positions
State the view of each imam: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (invalidation at exit only), Zufar (invalidation at entry only), Abu Yusuf (invalidation at both entry and exit).
These positions are all within the Hanafi school, representing different ijtihad.
3
Understand the conceptual basis
The commentator explains that attributing invalidation to exit is metaphorical. In reality, the chronic hadath was latent and manifests at exit because the license ends. This reconciles the exit-only view with the fact that the hadath is continuous.
This metaphor is key to understanding why the exit triggers invalidation.
4
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply each view to practical scenarios: e.g., using wudu from the forenoon (ḍuḥā) for Dhuhr, or using wudu from Fajr for 'Eid. Note which prayers are valid according to each position.
The thamarah shows the real impact of the dispute on daily worship.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates 'on the correct view' (على الصحيح), which is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (invalidation at exit only). This is the relied-upon ruling for fatwa.
Later Hanafi scholars have confirmed this as the operative view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويبطل وضوء المعذورين) إذا لم يطرأ ناقض غير العذر بخروج الوقت كطلوع الشمس في الفجر عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد (فقط) وعند زفر بدخوله فقط وقال أبو يوسف بهما وإضافة النقض للخروج مجاز وفي الحقيقة ظهور الحدث السابق به فيصلي الظهر بوضوء الضحى والعيد على الصحيح خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وزفر ولا يصلي العيد بوضوء الصبح خلافاً لزفر
His saying: 'And the wudu of those with chronic excuses becomes invalid' if no nullifier other than the excuse occurs, by the exit of the time, like the rising of the sun at Fajr, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (only). According to Zufar, it becomes invalid only by entry of the time. And Abu Yusuf said: by both. The attribution of invalidation to exit is metaphorical; in reality it is the manifestation of the previous hadath due to it. So he prays Dhuhr with the wudu of the forenoon (ḍuḥā) and [the prayer of] 'Eid on the correct view, contrary to Abu Yusuf and Zufar. And he does not pray 'Eid with the wudu of Fajr, contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding when the wudu of a person with a chronic excuse (ma'dhur) becomes invalid. The base text states that the wudu becomes invalid when the time for which he performed wudu exits, e.g., at sunrise for Fajr. However, there are three positions: (1) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: invalidation only at exit of time. (2) Zufar: invalidation only at entry of the new time. (3) Abu Yusuf: invalidation at both entry and exit. The commentator explains that the attribution of invalidation to exit is metaphorical: the actual nullifier (the chronic hadath) has been latent, and it manifests and becomes a legal impurity when the time exits, because the license (rukhṣah) ends. A practical consequence: if one prays Fajr with wudu made after the last third of the night, and then performs wudu again for the forenoon (ḍuḥā) to pray Dhuhr, the correct view (according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the Dhuhr prayer with that wudu is valid, contrary to Abu Yusuf and Zufar (who would consider the wudu already invalidated at entry of Dhuhr time). Also, 'Eid prayer cannot be prayed with the wudu of Fajr according to Abu Hanifa, whereas Zufar allows it (since he invalidates only at entry, but 'Eid time begins after sunrise).

Key Terms
خروج الوقت (khurūj al-waqt): The end of the prayer time, e.g., sunrise for Fajr, which causes invalidation of the ma'dhur's wudu according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
دخول الوقت (dukhūl al-waqt): The start of the prayer time; for Zufar, this alone invalidates the previous wudu, while for Abu Yusuf it is one of two points of invalidation.
ظهور الحدث السابق (ẓuhūr al-ḥadath al-sābiq): The manifestation of the pre-existing hadath (the chronic outflow) at the exit of time, metaphorically causing invalidation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, when does the wudu of a person with a chronic excuse for Fajr prayer become invalid?
A) At the entry of Fajr time
B) At the rising of the sun (exit of Fajr time)
C) At both entry and exit
D) It never becomes invalid as long as the excuse exists

Key Takeaway
The wudu of a ma'dhur is valid for the entire prayer time and becomes invalid at its exit, not at entry, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Washing Impurity from a Ma'dhur's Clothing
Intra-Hanafi dispute on washing ma'dhur's clothing impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on washing impurity from a ma'dhur's clothing and evaluate the different opinions within the school.

Core Idea
A ma'dhur's clothing impurity is excused only if it recurs; otherwise, washing is obligatory if it exceeds a dirham.

This lesson explores a nuanced branch issue in Hanafi taharah: whether a person with a chronic excuse (ma'dhur) must wash the impurity resulting from that excuse from his clothing before prayer. The passage presents multiple intra-Hanafi views, a critique, and a preferred ruling based on the concept of 'beneficial washing' (mufīd).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
State the question: Is the ma'dhur required to wash the najāsah of his excuse from his clothing?
This is a far' (branch) building on the principle that najāsah on the body is excused.
2
List the positions
Identify three views: (a) Ibn Salamah: no obligation; (b) mashāyikhunā: obligatory if >1 dirham and beneficial; (c) Ibn Muqātil: always obligatory by analogy to wudu.
The mashāyikhunā's view is the muftā bihī.
3
Analyze the key concept: 'beneficial washing'
Understand 'mufīd': if washing will immediately cause re-soiling, it is not beneficial, and the impurity is excused. Otherwise, it must be washed.
This avoids endless washing and hardship.
4
Examine the critique by Ibn Amīr Ḥājj
He objects using the analogy of the mustaḥāḍah, who is not commanded to wash her blood from clothing, implying no obligation for any recurring najāsah from an excuse.
This critique challenges the chosen view but does not overturn it.
5
Determine the muftā bihī and its thamarah
The chosen view (mukhtār) from al-Nawāzil: if washing leads to re-soiling before prayer ends, it is permissible not to wash; otherwise, it is obligatory.
The practical difference is whether the impurity recurs immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع : إذا أصاب ثوب المعذور نجاسة عذره هل يجب غسله قيل: لا لأن الوضوء عرف بالنص، والنجاسة ليست في معناه لأن قليلها يعفى فألحق به الكثير للضرورة ولأنه غير ناقض للوضوء، فلم يكن نجساً حكماً ولأن أمر الثوب ليس بآكد من البدن، وهو قول ابن سلمة: كما في القهستاني وغيره، وفي البدائع يجب غسل الزائد عن الدرهم إن كان مفيداً بأن لا يصيبه مرة بعد أخرى حتى لو لم يغسل، وصلى لا يجزيه وإن لم يكن مفيداً لا يجب ما دام العذر قائماً، اختيار مشايخنا اهـ وكان محمد بن مقاتل الرازي يقول : يجب غسله في كل وقت قياساً على الوضوء والصحيح قول مشايخنا لأن حكم الحدث عرف بالنص، والنجاسة ليست في معناه ألا ترى أن القليل منها عفو فلا تلحق به وفي النوازل إن كان لو غسله تنجس ثانياً قبل الفراغ من الصلاة جاز أن لا يغسله وإلا فلا قال وهو المختار اهـ قال ابن أمير حاج، ويشكل عليه ما قدمناه عن البدائع، وفي المضمرات في فصل الاستنجاء عن النوازل أيضاً المستحاضة إذا توضأت لوقت كل صلاة لا يجب عليها الاستنجاء إذا لم يكن منها غائط لأنه سقط اعتبار نجاسة دمها لمكان العذر اهـ فهذا أيضاً يشكّل على ما اختاره إذ سقوط إعتبار نجاسة دمها عام في البدن والثوب دفعاً للحرج إذ لم يأمرها بغسله، وتأخير البيان عن وقت الحاجة لا يجوز .
Branch: If the clothing of a person with a chronic excuse is struck by the impurity of his excuse, is it obligatory to wash it? It is said: No, because wudu was established by textual evidence, and impurity is not in its meaning, because a small amount of it is excused, so a large amount is analogized to it for necessity, and because it is not a nullifier of wudu, so it is not legally impure, and because the matter of clothing is not more stringent than the body. This is the saying of Ibn Salamah, as in al-Qahastānī and others. And in al-Badā'iʿ: It is obligatory to wash more than a dirham (in area) if it is beneficial, i.e., it will not be struck again and again. So if he does not wash it and prays, his prayer is not valid. And if it is not beneficial (i.e., it will recur immediately), it is not obligatory as long as the excuse persists. This is the choice of our scholars (mashāyikhunā). Muḥammad ibn Muqātil al-Rāzī used to say: It is obligatory to wash it every time by analogy to wudu. The correct view is the saying of our scholars because the ruling of hadath (legal impurity) was established by text, and impurity (najāsah) is not in its meaning. Do you not see that a small amount of it is excused, so it cannot be analogized to hadath. And in al-Nawāzil: If washing it would cause it to become impure again before finishing the prayer, it is permissible not to wash it, otherwise it is not. He said: and this is the chosen view. Ibn Amīr Ḥājj said: This is problematic given what we presented from al-Badā'iʿ. And in al-Muḍmarāt in the chapter of istinjā' from al-Nawāzil: The mustaḥāḍah, when she performs wudu for the time of each prayer, does not have to perform istinjā' if there is no stool, because the consideration of the impurity of her blood has fallen due to the excuse. This also challenges what he chose, since the dropping of consideration of the impurity of her blood applies to both body and clothing to remove hardship, as he did not command her to wash it. And delaying the explanation from the time of need is not permissible.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a fiqh discussion (far') regarding the obligation to wash the impurity of the chronic excuse from the clothing of a ma'dhur. Several positions are presented: (1) Ibn Salamah and others: no obligation, because the impurity of the excuse is treated like the impurity on the body, which is excused. (2) In al-Badā'iʿ: it is obligatory to wash amounts larger than a dirham if washing is beneficial (i.e., it will not be immediately re-soiled); if it will be re-soiled, it is not obligatory. This is the choice of the later Hanafi scholars (mashāyikhunā). (3) Muhammad ibn Muqātil al-Rāzī: obligatory by analogy to wudu. The text then mentions a view from al-Nawāzil that if washing would cause re-soiling before the prayer ends, it is permissible not to wash. The commentary by Ibn Amīr Ḥājj objects to the chosen view based on the ruling of the mustaḥāḍah in al-Muḍmarāt, where she is not required to wash if the impurity (blood) is from her excuse, and this applies to both body and clothing. Since the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not command her to wash, it indicates no obligation. The crucial point is that the ruling of najāsah (impurity) is different from the ruling of ḥadath (ritual impurity): ḥadath was established by text to require wudu, but najāsah is not analogized because a small amount is forgiven. Thus, the principle of necessity (ḍarūrah) supports excusing the impurity on clothing as well. The preferred view is that if the impurity is such that it repeatedly occurs, no washing is required; if it is a one-time occurrence, washing is necessary if it exceeds the amount of a dirham.

Key Terms
نجاسة عذر (najāsat 'udhr): The impurity that results from a chronic excuse (e.g., urine, blood). It is treated with leniency due to the excuse.
درهم (dirham): A unit of area roughly the size of a silver coin; used as a measure for the amount of impurity that is excused or requires washing.
مفيد (mufīd): Beneficial, i.e., washing the impurity will not immediately result in re-soiling; if it will, washing is not beneficial and not required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (al-mukhtār), when is a person with a continuous nosebleed (ru'āf) not required to wash the blood from his clothing?
A) Always, because he is excused
B) When the amount of blood is less than a dirham
C) When washing would cause the nose to bleed again before finishing the prayer
D) When the blood is dry

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a ma'dhur is excused from washing the impurity of his excuse from clothing only when washing is not beneficial (immediate re-soiling); otherwise, it remains obligatory if it exceeds a dirham.
