Wudu of the Ma'dhur: Invalidation, Conditions, and Clothing
Detailed exposition of rulings for those with chronic excuses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on wudu invalidation for the ma'dhur, identify the three conditions for ma'dhur status, and apply the ruling on washing clothes soiled with the excuse impurity.

Core Idea
The ma'dhur's wudu ends when the prayer time exits; chronic excuses require full-time encompassment, and garment impurity need not be washed if recurring.

This lesson dissects a dense passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi on the rulings for those with continuous excuses (ma'dhur). We will explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on wudu invalidation, the strict conditions for acquiring ma'dhur status, and the practical ruling on whether such a person must wash his garments. The discussion involves metaphorical interpretation, analogical reasoning, and careful balancing of hardship (haraj).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on wudu invalidation
Pinpoint the moment when the ma'dhur's wudu becomes invalid. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say at exit of the prayer time (e.g., sunrise for Fajr); Zufar says at entry; Abu Yusuf says both. Note the metaphorical attribution: nullification is attributed to exit, but in reality it is the manifestation of the previous hadath.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the correct view is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
Understand the conditions for being a ma'dhur
Learn the three conditions: (1) Ist'itāb (encompassment) of the excuse over a complete prayer time without interruption sufficient for wudu and prayer – real or legal (brief interruption). (2) Continuity: the excuse must persist after that initial encompassment (even if once, to know it remains). (3) Cessation: the ma'dhur ceases to be ma'dhur if a complete time passes with no excuse at all.
‘Time’ here refers to the time of the obligatory prayer.
3
Examine the ruling on new similar events
If a new event of the same type occurs (e.g., blood from another wound), it nullifies wudu because it is a new hadath, not a continuation of the excuse.
This shows the excused impurities only apply to the original excuse; new occurrences break wudu.
4
Analyze the ruling on washing clothes
If the ma'dhur's garment is soiled with the excuse impurity, the preferred view (mashayikh) is that washing is not obligatory if the impurity is recurring (i.e., the garment would become impure again before finishing prayer). If washing is beneficial (infrequent recurrence), it is obligatory to wash more than a dirham. The opposing view (Muhammad bin Muqatil) analogizes to wudu and requires washing every time.
The evidence is based on necessity and avoiding hardship, as seen in the mustahadah ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل وضوء المعذورين إذا لم يطرأ ناقض غير العذر بخروج الوقت كطلوع الشمس في الفجر عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد فقط وعند زفر بدخوله فقط وقال أبو يوسف بهما وإضافة النقض للخروج مجاز وفي الحقيقة ظهور الحدث السابق به فيصلي الظهر بوضوء الضحى والعيد على الصحيح خلافاً لأبي يوسف وزفر ولا يصلي العيد بوضوء الصبح خلافاً لزفر. ولا يصير من ابتلي بناقض معذوراً حتى يستوعبه العذر وقتاً كاملاً ليس فيه انقطاع لعذره بقدر الوضوء والصلاة إذ لو وجد لا يكون معذوراً وهذا الاستيعاب الحقيقي بوجود العذر في جميع الوقت والاستيعاب الحكمي بالانقطاع القليل الذي لا يسع الطهارة والصلاة شرط ثبوته أي العذر وشرط دوامه أي العذر وجوده أي والانقطاع. وشرعاً والمراد بالوقت وقت الفريضة قوله : إذا لم يطرأ ناقض غير العذر فإن طرأ ولو كان نظير عذره نقضه حتى لو كان به دماً ميل أو جدري فتوضأ وبعضها سائل، ثم سال الذي لم يكن سائلاً انتقض وضوءه لأنّ هذا حدث جديد فصار كما لو سال أحد منخريه فتوضأ مع سيلانه، وصلى ثم سال المنخر الآخر في الوقت انتقض وضوءه لأنّ هذا حدث جديد كما في الفتح. قوله : عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد متعلق بقوله : يبطل بعد تعلق قوله : بخروجه به. وهو فرع : إذا أصاب ثوب المعذور نجاسة عذره هل يجب غسله قيل: لا لأن الوضوء عرف بالنص، والنجاسة ليست في معناه لأن قليلها يعفى فألحق به الكثير للضرورة ولأنه غير ناقض للوضوء، فلم يكن نجساً حكماً ولأن أمر الثوب ليس بآكد من البدن، وهو قول ابن سلمة: كما في القهستاني وغيره، وفي البدائع يجب غسل الزائد عن الدرهم إن كان مفيداً بأن لا يصيبه مرة بعد أخرى حتى لو لم يغسل، وصلى لا يجزيه وإن لم يكن مفيداً لا يجب ما دام العذر قائماً، اختيار مشايخنا اهـ وكان محمد بن مقاتل الرازي يقول : يجب غسله في كل وقت قياساً على الوضوء والصحيح قول مشايخنا لأن حكم الحدث عرف بالنص، والنجاسة ليست في معناه ألا ترى أن القليل منها عفو فلا تلحق به وفي النوازل إن كان لو غسله تنجس ثانياً قبل الفراغ من الصلاة جاز أن لا يغسله وإلا فلا قال وهو المختار اهـ قال ابن أمير حاج، ويشكل عليه ما قدمناه عن البدائع، وفي المضمرات في فصل الاستنجاء عن النوازل أيضاً المستحاضة إذا توضأت لوقت كل صلاة لا يجب عليها الاستنجاء إذا لم يكن منها غائط لأنه سقط اعتبار نجاسة دمها لمكان العذر اهـ فهذا أيضاً يشكّل على ما اختاره إذ سقوط إعتبار نجاسة دمها عام في البدن والثوب دفعاً للحرج إذ لم يأمرها بغسله، وتأخير البيان عن وقت الحاجة لا يجوز .
The wudu of those with chronic excuses becomes invalid if no other nullifier besides the excuse occurs, by the exit of the time (e.g., sunrise for Fajr) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad only, while according to Zufar it becomes invalid only by the entry of the time, and Abu Yusuf said by both. The attribution of nullification to exit is metaphorical; in reality, it is the manifestation of the previous event. Thus one prays Dhuhr with the wudu of Duha and Eid prayers with it according to the correct view, contrary to Abu Yusuf and Zufar. And one does not pray Eid with the wudu of Fajr, contrary to Zufar. And one afflicted with a nullifier does not become a ma'dhur until the excuse encompasses a complete time without interruption, [the time] sufficient for wudu and prayer. If it exists, he is not ma'dhur. This real encompassing is by the existence of the excuse in all the time, and the legal encompassing is by the brief interruption that does not allow purification and prayer. This is a condition for its (the excuse's) establishment, and a condition for its continuity is its existence in every time after that real or legal encompassing, even if its existence is once to know its persistence, and a condition for its cessation and the exit of its owner from being ma'dhur is a complete time empty of it by its actual cessation. So these are three conditions: establishment, continuity, and cessation. And legally, what is meant by 'time' is the time of the obligatory prayer. His statement: 'if no other nullifier besides the excuse arises' — if it arises, even if it is similar to his excuse, it nullifies his wudu. So if he has a blood lump or smallpox and he performs wudu while some of it is oozing, then what was not oozing starts oozing, his wudu is nullified because this is a new event, just as if one nostril oozed and he performed wudu while it was oozing and prayed, then the other nostril started oozing within the time, his wudu is nullified because this is a new event, as in al-Fath. His statement: 'according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad' is attached to his statement 'becomes invalid' after attaching his statement 'by its exit'. And a subsidiary issue: If the ma'dhur's garment is soiled with the impurity of his excuse, must he wash it? It was said: No, because wudu is known by text, and impurity is not analogized to it because a small amount is excused, so a large amount is annexed to it for necessity, and because it does not nullify wudu, so it is not legally impure, and because the matter of the garment is not more serious than the body. This is the statement of Ibn Salama, as in al-Qahistani and others. In al-Bada'i', it is obligatory to wash more than a dirham if it is beneficial (i.e., if it does not recur repeatedly), so if he does not wash and prays, it does not suffice. If it is not beneficial, it is not obligatory as long as the excuse persists—this is the choice of our mashayikh. Muhammad bin Muqatil al-Razi said: It is obligatory to wash it every time by analogy with wudu. The correct is the statement of our mashayikh because the ruling of hadath is known by text, and impurity is not in its meaning; do you not see that a small amount is excused, so it is not annexed to it. In al-Nawazil: If washing it would cause it to become impure again before finishing the prayer, it is permissible not to wash it; otherwise, no. He said: This is the chosen view. Ibn Amir Hajj said: This is problematic based on what we mentioned from al-Bada'i'. And in al-Mudmarat, in the section on istinja' from al-Nawazil also: The mustahada who performs wudu for the time of each prayer is not required to perform istinja' if there is no feces from her, because the consideration of the impurity of her blood is dropped due to the excuse. So this also poses a problem for what he chose, since the dropping of consideration of her blood impurity is general to the body and garment to avoid hardship, as he did not command her to wash it. And delaying explanation beyond the time of need is not permissible.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage from Hashiya Al-Tahtawi is a detailed commentary on the rulings for those with chronic excuses (ma'dhur), such as those suffering from constant nosebleeds or urinary incontinence. The passage covers three main topics: (1) the invalidation of wudu at the exit of the prayer time according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, with differing views from Zufar and Abu Yusuf; (2) the conditions for being considered a ma'dhur—namely, that the excuse must encompass a full prayer time without interruption, and the conditions of establishment, continuity, and cessation; and (3) a subsidiary ruling on whether the ma'dhur must wash his clothes if soiled with his excuse impurity. The commentary presents various opinions, including those of Ibn Salama, the mashayikh, and Muhammad bin Muqatil al-Razi, ultimately choosing the view of the mashayikh that washing is not obligatory if the impure garment is quickly re-soiled, except where it is not beneficial. The passage also notes objections from Ibn Amir Hajj and examples from al-Mudmarat. The authority weight is from the later works, but the core ruling on wudu invalidation is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
معذور (ma'dhur): A person afflicted with a continuous excuse (e.g., constant bleeding) that hinders purification.
استيعاب (isti'āb): Encompassment of the excuse over a full prayer time, either real (continuous) or legal (brief interruptions).
خروج الوقت (khurūj al-waqt): The exit of the prayer time, e.g., sunrise for Fajr, which nullifies the ma'dhur's wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), which of the following does NOT invalidate the wudu of a ma'dhur?
A) The exit of the prayer time
B) A new event of the excuse type (e.g., another wound starts bleeding)
C) The entry of the next prayer time
D) Actually, both entry and exit invalidate according to Abu Yusuf, but not according to the correct view

Key Takeaway
The wudu of a ma'dhur is valid only until the exit of the prayer time; chronic excuses require full time encompassment, and garment impurity need not be washed if it recurs quickly.

Why Ritual Impurity Comes First: Rationale and Objections
Introduction explaining ordering of impurity types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi fiqh texts discuss ritual impurity before real impurity, and respond to the two main objections raised against this ordering.

Core Idea
Ritual impurity is discussed first because any remaining trace blocks prayer, while a little real impurity is excused.

In this opening of the chapter on impurities, the author establishes a key ordering principle: ritual impurity (hadath) takes precedence over real impurity (najasa). He then defends this ordering against two hypothetical objections, demonstrating the methodological care of Hanafi scholarship. This lesson unpacks the rationale and the objections, giving you insight into how fiqh texts structure their arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types of Impurity
Distinguish between ritual impurity (hadath, removed by wudu/ghusl) and real impurity (najasa, physical filth). Note their different legal implications.
Hadath prevents prayer until removed; najasa is excused in small amounts.
2
Analyze the Reason for Precedence
The author states that the prohibition of prayer remains as long as any part of the hadath persists, even a small amount, unless the remover (water/tayammum) reaches it. This is why it is discussed first.
Contrast with najasa: a little is excused, so it is less pressing.
3
Evaluate the First Objection
Consider the case of a person with amputated hands and injured face who prays without any purification. This seems to contradict the necessity of removing hadath. The answer: such cases are rare (nādir) and not a basis for general ruling.
General rulings are built on common occurrences, not exceptions.
4
Evaluate the Second Objection
If someone has both hadath and najasa but only enough water for one, he uses it for najasa. Does this imply najasa is stronger? The answer: he uses it for najasa so he can then perform tayammum for hadath, achieving both purities. The order is practical, not based on severity.
The goal is to achieve both purities, not to prioritize the more severe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الانجاس والطهارة عنها لما فرغ من بيان النجاسة الحكمية والطهارة عنها شرع في بيان الحقيقية ومزيلها وتقسيمها، ومقدار المعفو منها وكيفية تطهير محلها وقدمت الأولى لبقاء المنع عن المشروط بزوالها ببقاء بعض المحل وإن قل من غير إصابة مزيلها بخلاف الثانية، فإن قليلها عفو، بل قوله: (ومقدار المعفو منها وكيفية تطهير محلها فإنها تارة تكون بالدلك، وتارة بالمسح وغير ذلك. قوله : (وقدمت الأولى الخ) اعترض بالأقطع إذا كان مجروح الوجه فإنه يصلي بغير طهارة، وأجيب بأنه نادر فلا يبنى عليه حكم واعترض أيضاً بأن من به نجاسة وهو محدث إذا وجد ماء يكفي لأحدهما فقط يصرفه للنجاسة دون الحدث، فهذا يدل على ان النجاسة أقوى، وأجيب بأنه إنما أمر بصرفه للنجاسة ليتيمم بعده فيكون محصلاً للطهارتين لا لأنها أغلظ . قوله: (بزوالها ببقاء بعض المحل الجار الأول متعلّق بالمشروط، والثاني ببقاء المنع وقوله من غير
Chapter of Impurities and Purification Therefrom. When he finished explaining ritual impurity (najasa hukmiyya) and purification from it, he began explaining real impurity (najasa haqiqiyya), its removers, its divisions, the amount excused from it, and how to purify its location. And he gave precedence to the first (ritual impurity) because the prohibition of what is conditional upon its removal remains by the persistence of some of the location, even if little, without the remover reaching it, contrary to the second, for a little of it is excused. Rather, his statement: 'and the amount excused from it and how to purify its location, for sometimes it is by rubbing, sometimes by wiping, etc.' His statement: 'and he gave precedence to the first, etc.' was objected to by the one with no hands and an injured face, for he prays without purity. The answer is that it is rare, so no ruling is built upon it. It was also objected that one who has impurity and is in a state of hadath, if he finds enough water for only one of them, he uses it for the impurity rather than the hadath. This indicates that impurity is stronger. The answer is that he is only commanded to use it for impurity so that he can perform tayammum afterwards, thus achieving both purities, not because it is more severe. His statement: 'by its removal by the persistence of some of the location' — the first particle is attached to 'conditional', and the second to 'persistence of the prohibition', and his statement 'without'.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new chapter on real impurities (najasat) and their purification. The author explains why he discussed ritual impurity (hadath) before real impurity: because ritual impurity, once it occurs, prevents the validity of acts like prayer unless completely removed; even a small remaining part of the impurity disrupts the condition. In contrast, a small amount of real impurity (najasa) is excused. The commentary then addresses two objections to this ordering. The first objection: a person with amputated hands and an injured face who cannot perform wudu or tayammum still prays without any purity, which suggests ritual impurity is not always a barrier. The answer: such cases are rare and do not form the basis of a general ruling. The second objection: if a person has both hadath and najasa but only enough water for one, he uses it for najasa, indicating najasa is more serious. The answer: he uses it for najasa so that he can do tayammum for hadath, thereby achieving both purities, not because najasa is stronger. This passage sets the conceptual framework for the detailed rulings that follow.

Key Terms
النجاسة الحكمية (al-najāsa al-ḥukmiyya): Ritual impurity (hadath) that prevents prayer; removed by wudu or ghusl.
النجاسة الحقيقية (al-najāsa al-ḥaqīqiyya): Actual physical impurity (e.g., blood, urine) that must be removed from body and clothes.
معفو (ma'fū): Excused amount of real impurity allowed in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the author discuss ritual impurity (hadath) before real impurity (najasa)?
A) Because ritual impurity is more serious
B) Because real impurity is always excused
C) Because the prohibition related to ritual impurity remains even with a small remaining part
D) Because it is easier to explain

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ritual impurity is prioritized in discussion because its removal is unconditional for prayer validity, whereas real impurity is excused in small amounts.

Conditions for the Excuse (Ma'dhur) to Cease
Clarifies when a ma'dhur ceases to be excused

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the scenarios in which a ma'dhur ceases to be excused and apply the rule that the second prayer time is the criterion.

Core Idea
A ma'dhur is no longer excused only when the excuse ceases for an entire prayer time, judged by the second time.

We often think that once the bleeding stops, the person instantly becomes normal. But Hanafi fiqh is precise: the excuse status ends only when a complete prayer time passes without the excuse occurring. Furthermore, the retrospective effect depends on the continuity of cessation through the second time. Let's unpack these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: when does a ma'dhur cease to be excused? The ruling: a complete time free from the excuse ends the status.
The ma'dhur's status itself is defined by the excuse occurring in every entire prayer time.
2
Locate the authority
The ruling is drawn from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Al-Mudmarat, which are core texts of the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah).
These are authoritative sources for the mufta bihi view.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The reasoning: if a whole prayer time passes without the excuse, the condition for being ma'dhur (continuous occurrence) is broken. Hence, the person is no longer excused prospectively.
Retroactive effect depends on continuity through the second time.
4
Apply the scenarios
Work through the given scenarios: e.g., praying on cessation vs flow, and the second time being free or not. The key is that the second time determines the status.
If cessation lasts through second time, first prayer must be repeated; if excuse returns in second time, first prayer remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخروج صاحبه عن كونه معذوراً (خلو وقت كامل عنه بانقطاعه حقيقة فهذه الثلاث شروط الثبوت والدوام، والانقطاع نسأل الله العفو والعافية بمنه وكرمه.
قوله : (خلو وقت كامل عنه بإنقطاعه) فلو انقطع العذر في خلال الوقت فتوضأ وصلى على الانقطاع فيهما ودام الإنقطاع، فالصلاة صحيحة، ولا يعيد شيئاً، ولو توضأ وصلى على السيلان، ثم انقطع ودام الانقطاع فالصلاة صحيحة أيضاً ولا يعيد شيئاً لأنه معذور صلى صلاة المعذورين، ولو توضأ على الإنقطاع، وصلى على السيلان فكذلك لا يعيد شيئاً، ولو توضأ على السيلان، وصلى على الإنقطاع ودام الانقطاع، حتى خرج الوقت إنتقض الوضوء بخروج الوقت على ما يأتي فيتوضأ في الوقت الثاني، فإذا دام الإنقطاع حتى دخل الثالث أعاد الصلاة الأولى لأنه أداها بطهارة المعذورين والعذر زائل ولا يعيد الصلاة الثانية لأن فساد الأولى إنما عرف بعد خروج الثانية، فلم يجب الترتيب، ولم ينتقض وضوء بدخول الوقت الثالث لأنه صار صحيحاً . أفاده صاحب البحر وصاحب المضمرات ولو طراً العذر في خلال الوقت. قال في الظهيرية : رجل رعف، أو سال جرحه ينتظر آخر الوقت فإن انقطع الدم فيها وإن لم ينقطع توضأ وصلى قبل خروج الوقت فإذا فعل، ثم دخل وقت صلاة أخرى ثانية وإنقطع ودام الانقطاع إلى وقت صلاة أخرى ثالثة أعاد الصلاة يعني الأولى التي صلاها مع السيلان لأنه بدوام الانقطاع تبين أنه صحيح صلى صلاة المعذورين وإن لم ينقطع في وقت الصلاة الثانية حتى خرج الوقت جازت الصلاة لأنه تبين أنه معذور كما في البحر والحاصل أن الوقت الثاني هو المعتبر في إثبات العذر وعدمه.
And his exiting from being excused (a complete time free from it, by its actually ceasing; so these are the three conditions: establishment, continuity, and cessation; we ask Allah for pardon and well-being through His grace and generosity).
His saying: (a complete time free from it, by its ceasing). If the excuse ceases during the time, and he performs wudu and prays relying on the cessation in both, and the cessation continues, then the prayer is valid and he does not repeat anything. If he performs wudu and prays relying on the flow, then the excuse ceases and the cessation continues, the prayer is also valid and he does not repeat anything because he is excused and prayed the prayer of the excused. If he performs wudu on cessation and prays on the flow, similarly he does not repeat anything. If he performs wudu on the flow and prays on the cessation and the cessation continues until the time exits, then the wudu is nullified by the exit of the time as will come; so he performs wudu in the second time. If the cessation continues until the third time enters, he repeats the first prayer because he performed it with the purity of the excused while the excuse had ceased. He does not repeat the second prayer because the invalidity of the first was only known after the second exited, so sequential order is not required, and the wudu is not nullified by the entry of the third time because it became valid. This is reported by the author of Al-Bahr and the author of Al-Mudmarat. If the excuse occurs during the time: It is said in Al-Zahiriyya: A man who has a nosebleed or bleeding from a wound waits until the end of the time. If the blood stops during it, even if it does not stop, he performs wudu and prays before the time exits. If he does, then another time for the second prayer enters and the excuse stops and the cessation continues until the time of a third prayer, he repeats the prayer, meaning the first which he prayed with the flow, because by the continuation of cessation it becomes clear that he was sound and prayed the prayer of the excused. If it does not stop during the second time until the time exits, the prayer is valid because it becomes clear that he was excused, as in Al-Bahr. The conclusion is that the second time is what is considered for proving the excuse or its absence.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with detailed scenarios from the commentary on Maraqi al-Falah. It addresses the conditions under which a person who suffers from a chronic excuse (like continuous nosebleed or wound bleeding) ceases to be considered excused (ma'dhur). The key condition for the excuse to end is that a complete prayer time passes without any occurrence of the excuse (e.g., no bleeding). Three conditions are implied: the excuse must have been established initially, it must have continued, and then it must cease completely. The commentary then outlines multiple scenarios. If the excuse stops during a time, and the person prays relying on that cessation, and the cessation continues, the prayer is valid. If they prayed while the excuse was still present (relying on flow), and it stops later, the prayer remains valid because they acted according to their status as excused. Crucially, if they pray on cessation but the excuse returns later, the ruling depends on the second time period: if the cessation lasts through the entire second time, then the person is deemed not excused retroactively, and they must repeat the first prayer. However, the second prayer remains valid because its invalidity was not known until after its time. The general principle is that the second time period is the decisive factor: if the excuse ceases for the whole second time, the person is no longer excused; if it returns during the second time, they remain excused. All scenarios are based on Hanafi fiqh as transmitted in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and Al-Mudmarat. This is a good example of the Hanafi precision in taharah rulings for the excused.

Key Terms
معذور (ma‘dhūr): A person who has a continuous excuse (e.g., nosebleed, urine incontinence) that prevents wudu from remaining intact for the entire prayer time.
خلو وقت كامل (khalā' waqt kāmil): The complete absence of the excuse during an entire prayer time, which ends the excuse status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for proving the existence or absence of the excuse?
A) The first prayer time after the excuse begins.
B) The second prayer time after the initial one.
C) The entire day
D) The time when the person prays

Key Takeaway
A person ceases to be excused (ma'dhur) only when a complete prayer time passes without the excuse, and the second time is the decisive criterion for retroactive effect.

Definition and Division of Real Impurity
Defines real impurity and divides it into heavy and light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define real impurity (najāsa ḥaqīqiyya), distinguish between najas and najis, and state the basis for dividing real impurity into heavy and light.

Core Idea
Real impurity divides into heavy and light by amount excused, not by purification method.

This lesson opens the chapter on real impurities. After covering ruling impurity (hadath), the text now turns to physical impurities that affect the validity of worship. Understanding the precise definition and classification is essential for knowing which impurities are excused and how to purify them.

Guided Path
1
Define real impurity
Identify the definition: a substance considered filth by Islamic law (shar'an). Contrast it with ruling impurity (hadath) which is a state, not a substance.
Real impurity is physically present; ruling impurity is a legal state.
2
Distinguish najas vs. najis
Learn the linguistic difference: najas (fatha) is a noun for an inherently impure substance; najis (kasra) is an adjective for something that has become impure. Use the Quranic verse 9:28 and the cat hadith as evidence.
This distinction matters for proper terminology in fiqh and fatwa.
3
Understand the division basis
The division into heavy (ghalīẓa) and light (khafīfa) is based on the amount excused in prayer, not on the method of purification. Heavy impurity: only less than a dirham's weight is excused; light impurity: more lenient.
The text explicitly states: 'bi-‘tibāri qillati al-ma‘fūwi ‘anhu, lā fī kayfiyyati taṭhīrihi'.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, determine if an impurity is heavy or light, then check if the amount exceeds the excused limit. Heavy examples: blood, wine, dog saliva. Light examples: urine of a male child who has not yet eaten solid food.
The exact thresholds are detailed in later lessons; here we focus on the conceptual division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on Impurities and Purification from Them
When he finished explaining the ruling impurity (hadath) and purification from it, he began explaining the real impurity (najasa haqiqiyya), its remover, its division, the amount excused from it, and the method of purifying its place. He gave precedence to the first (ruling impurity) because the prohibition of the conditional act remains due to its removal by the remaining of some part of the place, even if little, without the remover affecting it, unlike the second (real impurity) because a small amount of it is excused, rather much for necessity.
His saying: (and the method of purifying its place: sometimes by rubbing, sometimes by wiping, etc.) His saying: (and he gave precedence to the first, etc.) There is an objection concerning the amputee whose face is wounded: he prays without purification. The answer is that it is rare, so no ruling is built on it. There is also an objection that a person who has impurity (najasa) and also is in a state of hadath, if he finds enough water for only one of them, he uses it for the impurity rather than hadath. This indicates that impurity is stronger. The answer is that he is ordered to use it for the impurity so that he can perform tayammum afterwards, thus obtaining both purifications, not because impurity is more severe.
His saying: (by its removal with the remaining of some part of the place) the first preposition is related to 'the conditional', and the second to 'the prohibition remains'. His saying: without

[PAGE_152]
used
Book of Purification
much for necessity. And al-anjas is the plural of najis (with two fatha), a noun for an object considered filthy by law. Its origin is a verbal noun, then used as a noun in His saying: 'Indeed the idolaters are najas' (9:28). It is used for ruling (hukmi) and real (haqiqi) impurity. Al-khabath is specific to real, and al-hadath is specific to ruling. So najas (with fatha) is a noun and does not take the ta' marbuta, and with kasra it is an adjective and takes the ta'. And at-tathir is either proving purity in a place or removing impurity from it, and it is obligatory regarding that which is not excused. It has been narrated that the first thing a servant will be asked about in his grave is purity, and that most punishment of the grave is from lack of care for it and lack of caution against impurity, especially urine. He began explaining its reality and said: (The real impurity divides into two divisions: one is heavy impurity) according to the paucity of what is excused from it, not in the method of purifying it, because it does not differ due to heavy or light. (And) the second division
His saying: (falling upon) is related to 'the remaining of some part of the place'. His saying: (rather much for necessity) as when one has impurity on himself and cannot remove it except by uncovering himself in front of someone for whom uncovering is not permissible; then he prays with it even if it is a large amount. His saying: (plural of najis with two fatha) and other forms occur like rijs, kitif, 'udud, and fas. The verb is from the patterns fariha, kathuma, 'alima, and nasara. His saying: (considered filthy by law) — if he had omitted 'by law' it would be better because he is in the context of a linguistic definition, and what is in Al-Misbah and others is that it is used for everything filthy. His saying: (its origin is a verbal noun) — if it is said that a verbal noun is not dualized nor pluralized and is the same for masculine and feminine as in the verse and the hadith of the cat that it is not najis (with fatha of jim) as narrated by Malik, Ahmad, the authors of the Sunan, and al-Darimi — then how did the author pluralize it? The answer is that this is when the verbal noun remains in its verbal noun meaning because its reality is one with no multiplicity; but if he intends its types, as here, then its pluralization is permissible. His saying: (then it was used as a noun) i.e., for the filthy substance. His saying: ('Indeed the idolaters are najas') this is evidence for its being a verbal noun, so it should have been mentioned before his saying 'then it was used as a noun'. His saying: (and it is used) i.e., linguistically. His saying: (so najas with fatha is a noun, etc.) The jurists distinguished between the fatha and kasra forms: the former is something inherently impure, and is not said for that which has accidental impurity; the latter is that which is not pure, so it is more general absolutely. Thus, feces (adherent filth) is with both forms, while a garment that has become impure is only with kasra. His saying: (and tathir is either proving purity, etc.) It is said in the Sharh: According to both definitions, impurity is first established in the place, whether real or ruling, and the obligation is either affirming what is established (in the first) or removing what is removed (in the second). End. Paraphrased. His saying: (from lack of care for it, i.e., not knowing how to remove it) and 'al-ih tiraz' (caution) is coordinated with 'al-ihtimam' i.e., from lack of caution against impurity, i.e., from being hit by it, such as dragging one's garment so that impurity hits it. The coordination is then that of different things. His saying: (especially urine) for it has been narrated: 'Guard yourselves from urine, for most punishment of the grave is from it.' And it is mentioned that punishment of the grave is from three things: backbiting, slander, and not guarding from urine. His saying 'especially' is a verbal noun with 'urine' as its object, i.e., I specify urine as most punishment of the grave is from it. His saying: (and he began explaining its reality) meaning he did not mention here except some individuals of each, and the discussion of the reality will come from him and from them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on real impurities (najasat haqiqiyya) and purification from them. The author explains why he discussed ruling impurity (hadath) first: because even a small remaining part of the impurity prevents the validity of the conditional act (like prayer), unless the remover (water) actually reaches it. In real impurity, a small amount is excused, and even a large amount is excused in cases of necessity. The commentary then addresses several points: First, why precedence is given to ruling impurity over real; an objection about an amputee whose face is wounded—he prays without purification—is dismissed as rare. Another objection about a person who has both najasa and hadath but only enough water for one: they use it for the najasa, then do tayammum for hadath. This does not indicate najasa is more severe but is to achieve both purifications. The text then defines the term 'najas': it is the plural of 'najis' (with two fatha), originally a verbal noun meaning filth, used as a noun for an impure substance. Evidence from Quran 9:28 shows the verbal noun form. The distinction between 'najas' (with fatha) and 'najis' (with kasra) is explained: 'najas' is an inherent property of something impure, while 'najis' is an adjective for something that has become impure. Thus, feces can be called both, while a soiled garment is only 'najis'. Purification (tathir) is defined as either proving purity in a place or removing impurity. The passage ends with a strong encouragement to avoid impurity, especially urine, citing hadith about grave punishment. Finally, the division of real impurity into heavy (ghalizah) and light (khafifah) is introduced, based on the amount excused, not on the method of purification.

Key Terms
نجاسة حقيقية (najāsa ḥaqīqiyya): Real impurity, such as blood, urine, wine, etc., that is physically present on a substance.
نجس (najis (with kasra) / najas (with fatha)): With fatha: a noun meaning an inherently impure substance. With kasra: an adjective describing something that has become impure.
غليظة (ghalīẓa): Heavy impurity, of which only a very small amount (less than a dirham's weight) is excused in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for dividing real impurity into heavy and light according to the passage?
A) The method of purification (rubbing vs. washing).
B) The amount excused from it in prayer.
C) The source of the impurity (human vs. animal).
D) The color or smell of the impurity.

Key Takeaway
Real impurity is defined by shari'ah, divided into heavy and light based on the amount excused, with the linguistic distinction between najas (inherent) and najis (accidental) being key to correct usage.
